Fine Arts.—Amon" the pictures sold during the present exhibition at the British Gallery are—The Enchanted Island, by Mr. Danby, purchased by John Gibbons, Esq., for 200 guineas ; A Scene in Bristol Harbour, by G. Jones, R. A., for 100 guineas, by Sir Thomas Lawrence; Pandora, crowned by the Seasons, by SirEtty, for 158 guineas, to Sir Thomas Lawrence: The Champion, by Mr. Eastlake, for 150guineas, by Wm. Hutchinson, Esq. ; The Review, by Mr. Farrier, for 70 guineas, by P. Ellams, Esq.; Othello relating his History, by Mr. Fradelle, for 150 guineas, by John Marshall, Esq.; The Burial of Christ, by Mr. Northcote, R. Stamen’s Hospital Society.—This most benevolent institution celebrated its fourth anniversary festival yesterday, at the City of London Tavern. Wm. Manning, Esq., M.P., was called to the chair, in the absence of Ivord Melville, who was prevented from being present bv his public duties.. It was attended by ViceAdmiral Sir Richard King, Rear Admiral Douglas, Captain Sir G.Wcstpha!, R.N-, Captain Sir C. Sullivan, R.N., SirF. Ommanny, Bart., M.P., Sir Wm. Kay, Bart., the Rev. Sir 3. C. Jervoise. Sir Thomas Tyrwhitt, Sir Janies Little, and several officers Of the naw, and gentlemen of distinction. It appeared from the report of the "committee, that at the period when this charity was first instituted four years ago, the number of seamen destitute of medical advice, in the pottos London, was ascertained to be from 7 to 800. In that year (1821), 413 were received into the Hospital; since then the number has increased gradually; and last year it amounted to no less than 1,249; to support the expense of this number, however, amounting to 3,5001. a-year, the resources of the Society arc wholly inadequate,'—their funds consisting only of the interest of 3,0601. stock; and the annual subscriptions, about 1,0001. each. In the course of the evening several appeals in behalf of poor sick seamen were made to the humanity of the assembly and to the public in general; and more particularly to the consideration of the merchants, who, owing to the circumstances of the institution and its objects not being sufficiently known to them, had not yet qpmc forward with that liberality for which they arc so conspicious on all charitable occasions. It was therefore hoped, that as the institution would acquire notoriety, that alone being necessary to procure for it the countenance and support of every charitable individual, the contributions would increase, and the funds be thus rendered sufficient to continue the rcceptisn of the present number of poor seamen; and prevent the necessity of shutting the doors of the hospital in future upon so many of this most deserving class of men, and leaving them to their miserable fate at a time when they stood in greatest need of aid and attention. Amongst the subscriptions announced by the treasurer, many were from gentlemen of the East Indies.
The Recorder of London yesterday made his. report to the King in Council of the following prisoners under sentence of death in Newgate, and who were convicted at the December Old Bailey Sessions, namely—Lot Cartlidgc, aged 49; Mary Ann Rycroft, 35; Charles Metzger, 19; William Lilley, 21; Hannah Harriet Burton, 42; Elizabeth Bowslcy, 13; Caroline Molineux, 12; and William Harrison, 21; for stealing in a dwelling-house to the amount of 40s. and upwards: all of whom His Majesty was graciously pleased to respite during Iris Royal pleasure. The cases of Edward Harris, for highway robbery; Levi Abrahams, for burglary ; and Robert Ball, for arson, arc postponed until a future report". None of the cases in January sessions were reported.
An inquest was yesterday morning taken before T. Stirling, Esq., at St., Giles’s Workhouse, on the body of Joseph Keaton, whose death was the result of violence used towards him by his wife, and for which she is now in custody. Ths jury returned a verdict of “- Wilful murder against his wife, Mary Keaton."
A couple of villains, whose respectable appearance renders them unsuspected, have lately been practising an artful and cruel species of robbery. They are accustomed to visit various Almshouses, under colour of being employed by the trustees to inspect tlie buildings, for the purpose of ascertaining what repairs may be needful; and. in the course of their, pretended.' examinations, they take an opportunity of stealing any little valuable article which may come hi their way. Some of the aged tenants of she benevolent retreats which these wretches have visited with this infamous design, have formerly occupied respectable stations in lite, and" probably retain a few reuc* of value. At the Alms-houses, near hhoreditch church, one poor old woman lost a watch, and another, three silver spoons. Similar depredations were commit ed in the Borough and elsewhere. It would be highly gratifying to see these surveyors standing at the bar of a court of justice. * .

A collection of paintings, under the above description, by old mastcrs, opens to the public this day at Exeter-hall, in the Strand. We were admitted to aview of this collection on Saturday; and found it a strange assemblage of the valuable and the wortbless-a few fine, though not of the highest class, some rare, and really curious, mixed up.with -many that have no pretensions to such good company. Among the first we noticed an excellent Cnrc .ion, in ivory, -by Benvenuto Cellini4 a pajnting by Steenwyck, of the Deliuery of St. Peter from Prisont m which, though the colouring is very inferior, the grouping is masterly, and the air of stillness anud repose.which should. pervade the subject weU preserved throughout, a painting of Samson and Dalilah, by Jan Steen, which possesses mainy exceRencies, but, not'ithstandiug its charm of colour and natural execution, is vitiated by the boorish forms and coarse expressions the natural emanations of that painter's pencil; and an aliegorical painting by Ghirlandio, the master- of Michael Angelo, conspicuous for the glow of its colour. and natural expression. A painting, stated in the catalogue. to be by Albano, is-hung in so ill a light, that it is impossible to pass an opimnoR as to its merits, because it cannot be seen, Of the rest it is unnecessary to say much.. The collection will doubtless warm .the devotion of the pious, ar in some respects gtatify the curidsity of the antiquary, but it possesses fevr attractions for the lovers of the really beautiful in art.

ErXIIBITION OYfi ALL THE DESIOGS OFFI:gRED FOR THI NEIV HOUSES OP PARLIA.ENT, NOW BXrFLBIRTIZAG IN THB NATIONAL GALL ErVY.
The last article in this paper concluded a brief but for the present sufficient notice of Ai r. Barry's desian, tl the public are in possession of that gentleman's denuded altered, and in fact newly-made design, now in hand, or cotmpleted by order of the committce for rebuilding the two houses of Parliament. MIr. Barry's evidence about his design, hisreasons foraltering it, seting it further back from-the river, intruding 150 feet h2rther into Abingdon-street, enlarging hiis courts,and various other alteration*, are in printfor the use of the committee, hut the public and the profession will not be satisfied till more candour and less mystery is observed.
The architects complain that the first premium is awarded to an avowed protega of one of the commissioners, and the second to an artist who not only avowed his name by the anagram or hieroglyphic of a buckle and the letter R, but is in a similar relation to another of the commissioners, and that the other two designs are also vastly inferior to manv of those which the commissioners scornfully rejected. They also complain that the drawings of Air. Barry's design, wvhichl we have before praised in contradistinction to those of Mur. Buckler, am So minute and elaborate, that the commissioners have more than once alluded to them in terms of praise, whilc they censure some of those which they have rejected, for the introd!uction of profuse and unnecessary omamene, m;erely to bcautify their designs, btut which would, if executed, be a wasteful expendliture of public money; justifying in the same breath the enriched appearance of the one, and censuring it in thte others.
M'ith regard to the circumstance of Mlr. Barry being permitted by the commissioners or committee to alter his design, there can be no real ground of complaint. On the contrary, had they insisted on his keeping his entire design for a combination of buildings that cover an area of more than scven acres of ground intuct and unaltered, they would truly have deserved censure; for if they have conscientiously awarded the p.alm of merit to this design, and MIr.Barry, its author,be an architect of sufficient knowledge and practice in his profession, as none who know Itim can doubt, then he ought to be installed at once 'E-,-v,; v, E-e ,-the superintendent of the works, as Sir Christoliher WVren was of those of8t. Pauls Cathedral. and the other churches and public buildings of the city after the great lire of London. This greatest of Ettglislt architects, in spite of the sneers of Sir Joh'h Soane, that he would rather be the designer of lr.igo Jou,s's watergate at the hottom ot Buckinghatn-street than all that WVren ever did, was so installed by Charles IT., and in every commission for those works was formally acknowledged, and gazrtted as a brother commissioner Nvith the great, the noble, anid the leained in the land, and not deKraOed to the level of aservant. In this authoritative eapacity he was appointed under the great seal, dated, according to his biographer, Elmes. wlto gives the whole commission and names of the commissioners, Novcmber 1°, 1673, as srchitect and o0w of the commissionesi for rebuilding the Cathedral of St. Paul. Tite letters patent were issued unto several lords, spiritual and :empora:, and other Persons of high rank and quality, and to Christopiter Wren, Doctor of Lavs,Surveyor-Gcneral of the Royal Wjrorks, &c. This commission and letters patent would formn a good precedent for a similar commnission for re-building the Houses of Parliament.
This great architect was not only permitted to alter his design, butdidsofromtinie to time, as the worksarose, and his knowledge improved and it is curious to observe the pro. gress of alteration in this great man's mind and design, as enumerated in hkIr. Elmes's work, taken from the autograph cesizns of the architect preserved in the second volume of Sir Christopher lVren's deigns in the library of All Souls' Col. lege. Oxford. The King and his Government allowed and autlhorized the architect to mnake such variations and alterations in his design as he pleased, and most properly, as his hiographer observes, left the whole to his managemestt, a- far as the design went: hence the homogenieal style of this tunique and complete building; snch should be the constitution and conduLct of the commission for rebuilding the housce of Parliament, and if the selection of the architect be unobjectionable, trifling alterationis of detais and arrangemcnt ought to be per.mit ted.
Of the design which obtained thethird premium, itis difficult to conceive by what powers of ratiocination it can have been so honoured in the face of all the other surrounding designs. If Mr. Cockerell's beautiful design was degraded to a lowergrade than this, as bcing neither Elizabethan nor Gothic,what in the name of all fineart isthe style of this? If thi. design, and that wMhichl obtained the fourth premium, be so immeasurably above all those which are rejected, that the commissioners, who were desired to select not less than three nor more than five designs for them to honour in the face of the Btitish nation, could not find a fifth amonz the 80 other designs, wvliv let us, as Henry VIIL did, senI to Italy for architects to supply our own deficiency. But we are not so defective in architectural talent as these commissioners have declared,by reporting that they could not find a fifth worthy even to be placed below the two which are hung back to back in the second room of the National. Gallery, as if saying to them in proof of the greatness they have tlhus thrust upon them, si conforanotionern requiris, cireumspice.
It is well, however, that all the sueccssful, and most of the unsuccessful, designs are now before the public in this exhibition, together with those of Sir Johnt Snane, now exhibiting in the Royal Academy, designed in 1794 and 1796, and one made in Rome by the same architect in 1779, and those bv Mr. Gandy exhibited at tile Royal Academy in 1833 and 1135, a portion or which is also in the same exhibition and the designs published bythe House of Commons in 1833 miade by Sir John Soane, Sir-Jeffry Wvattville. and Mlesss. Benjamin Wvystt, Savage, the lae WVilliam Kent, Deering, [,ee, Blore, the Right Hon. J. W. Croker, Goodwin. Rigby Wason, MI.P., Decimus Burton, Allen, and Hanbury Tracey, Esq., AY.P., are all at once before the tribunal of public justice. The public in general are unsophisticated judges, and have always done justice, particularly in the fine arts, as history proves, from the timeof their just adiudication bltween Phidies and Alcamenes, as to their rivalstatues of Minerva for the Acrepolis, to the reversal of the decision of a late Pcpe who invited a competition of designs for the Piazza lel Popolo, in which our countryman, Harrison of Chester, embarkedi, and the judges selected Roman designs; but against which decision the eclebrated Piranesi protested * the public were admitted, and Harrison received the justly deserved prize.
Thepublicaremostly julst judges; Aristotle admits that the multitude are the surent judge of the productions of the arts, and Cicero says,that although the people may sometimes
dmircean inferior work, yet when it is placed beside a fitstare performance, they will not hesitate in giving a correct decision.

THEl BXHBITIION OF PICTUABPS AT T.IrB
LOUYVRE.
(PtOM A COBBBSPONDEipT.)
PAY-IS, MoNDAY. , I have just left the annual exhibition of pictures by living artists, wvhich opened this morning in the gallery of the Louvre, aud sit down to givTe you my first impressions of it, fresh as received. As usual upon this oventful day, the most important in the year to the numerous tribe of paintera abiding in Paris, the Place du Museum was crowded from a very early hour with artists and their friends. At 11 o'clock the doors opened, the gaUeries were filled in an instant, and continued so the whole day. It is amusing enough to see the eagerness of the artirts, many of whom, either not choosing to expend a franc on a catalogue, or too impatient to wfait their turn to obtain one froi the vendor, rush up and donm the galleries in a. state of frantic excitement, seeking the precious bit of canvas, which they will not be. lieve the jury bas beesa so injudicious as to reject. For a long time perhaps the search is in vain; at last the invaluable specimen of art flashes upon the delighted Apelles, cheering his eyes and heart, or else a peep at a friend's catalogue convinces him that he is not amongst the chosen. If curses were cannon balls, the committee would this day, Within a few minutes afterll, have found themselve3 particularly well pulverized, expese&as they were to the anathemas not only of the rejected but of the misplaced; of those whose picturel are hung too high or too low, in too strong a light or too feeble a one. Whether rejections have been less numerous, or the pictures submitted more so this year than last, I can. not say, but a greater number havo found favour in the eyes of the jury. To say nothing of sculpture, engramings, archi. tectural models, &c., upwards of 2,009 pictures, the pro. duction9 of some 1,200 artists, have been alh,wed places in tho Louvre.
To commence at the top of the tree, the point to which public attention is first directed:-AryScheffer, who maybe considerad thelcaderefthepresentFrenchschool,andwhofor the last year or two, in common with Ingres, P. Delaroche, and other eminent artists, has sent nothing to the exhibition,
now makes up for bis previous absonce by the contribution| of seven pictures-three religious subjects, three taken from Goethe, and a portrait. Of these pictures it may be said that all have considerable merit and showthe hand ofa s3killed artist, bu: that all have also important defects. Tie drawing is excellent,. but there is a want of roundn'ss in the figures,-an angularity arising partly perhaps from the imitation of the German school, at which Scheffer and his followers aim, but in which they are not always successful. uis colouring is decidedly bad-chalky and without depthand the general impression conveyed to me by his pictures is a want of vigour, lishbest this year iuWJ'Enfant 0haritable," a subject drawn from Goetz von Terlichingen. To Henry Seleffer it is impossible to give even the qualified praise bestowed upon his far more giftea brother. His heads of Christ are most unpleasing, not to say offensive; heavy, expressionless, and badly conceived. One is led to believe that he has taken for his model some bearded, leaden-visaged lounger from the Boulevard des Italiens; assuredly he has not sought his inspiration where best it might be found-in a careful study and contemplationof the old masters.
Of the square room preceding the long gallery, and where a majority of the larger and more remarkable pictures usually fin I place, nearly the wyhole of one side is occupied by an enormous and really fine picturo by Horace Vernetthe " Battle of Isly," painted for tho Versailles Gallery. Itis the moment -when Colonel Jousaouf presents to General Bugeaud the Moorish st-andards, and the Binperor of Mo. rocco's famous parasol, which ho and his Spahis hare cap.

Africa has also supplied Philippoteaux -with subjects for three agreeable pictures; the late Duke of Orleans restoring their lihberty to two Arab prisoners, "Moorish women in their apartment;" and "A Street in Algiers ;" the latter full of character, and of lazy Oriental faraiente.

The recent exchange of visits botween the Sovereigns of France sad England has supplied various painters with subjects, and it is to be wished theyhad handled them better. There are remarkably few landscapes this year; rather an advantage, when one remembers the vast n-amber of daubs of this description that usually encumber the walls of* the gaUery. Of the few that there are, but few are good; the remainder consisting of those masses of green styled by the French plates of spilach, more lke signs for a greengrocer's shop than specimens of art. Portraits, on the other hand, always a staple commodity, abound, and there is a fair proportion of good oues. Groselaude exhibits tw7o ladies, whose sweet countenances would pass off far worse paintings. Guignet, Perignon, and Heuss have several excellent portraits; one of M. Guizot by the last-named painter is a remarkably good likeness of that statesman. Lepaulle has made the most of the dull countenance of a Spanish grandee, the present Duke of Osuna. There are a few good miniatures, showing progress in this line of art. Whose by J. Gaye, on the right hand in the ante-chamber, are amongst the best, and are admirable for their careful finish. As usual, Madamo de Mirbel and her pupils muster in great force, and have the best places assigned to them.
Alfred Dedreux, the Prenich Landseer, although at a vast distance from his English contemporary, has some very good pictures, the best of them beink " a Hunting Party in the reign of Louis XV." This artist displays great skill in the distribution of his lights anud the general arrangement of his pictures; but his horses would be far better if their attitudes were less forced, and if he would allovw some of them to stand still, instead of twisting themselves about and vislently pawing, as if tormented by flies. He has a spirited group of dogs in the picture above referred to, although it seems miraculous that the piqueur vwho holds them should be able to resist the violent tugging of so many large and powerful hounds. Anywhere but in a picture he would assuredly be drageed forward, instead of holding them in, as M. Dedreux enables him to do, with perfect ease and comfort to himself. A little bit of tenderness, a lady amorously leaning upoll the shoulder of her attendant cavalier, might also have been spared, as hardly likely to be enacted, even iu the facile days of the lath Louis, in the midst of an assemblage. of grooms and huntsmen. But these trifling defects and affectations M. Dedreux vill doubtless learn to qorrect. Of other animal painters, Janet Lange may be named, although very inferior indeed to DedreuxAlady of the pleasant name of Rosa Bonheur has a very fair grotp of sheep reposing ; and Verboeckhoven, a Belgian artist, has a capital dog picture, the very best thing of the kind thatI saw in the exhibition. Be it observed, npa9ssant, thatlast year some of the best pictures were contributed by foreign artists, and this year Belgians, EDnglish, and a Spaniard cut a most respectable figure at the Louvre. A " Vierge Consolatrice," by Madamo Fanny Geefs, of Brussels, is a charming picture. Senor Villa Amil, who sent soime capital Spanish scenes to former exhibitions, has only one picture in the present year's show, but that one very good, " the Salon de los Embajadores, in the Madrid Palace." W. Jackson's "Fete sous Louis XV.," isWatteauishandagree able, although a little outnf. There is a view of the " Port of Ancona," by Stanfield ; but in the crowd and hurry of a fist visit I missed seeing it.
Amongst the marine painters, Grudin has sent ao less than 13 pictures, but unfortunately the quality by no means keeps pace with the quantity. This artist has, of late, greatly fallen off. The " Scene on the coast of Scotland," seafowl on a rock, &c., is perhaps one of the most respectable of his this year's productions. His sea-fights are very so-so. A MIr. Mayer contributes an episode of French naval history, interesting, if true; an English 50-gun ship boarded and t9ken by a French privateer, carrying 32 8-pounders. Another painter, nearly of the samO name, Meyer, has a good rescue of a shipwrecked lady by a Newfoundland dog; the water admirably transparent. Biard, whose pictures are generally favourites, has the "Right of Search," a slaver boarded by a French vessel, and the slaves set at liberty. His other picture, a shipwirecked group upon a raft attacked by a shark, drew a crowd of gazers. It is very spirited, although the shark appeared to me more like a vwhale.
There is, of course, a sprinkling of absurities in the exhibition, but reatUy only a sprinkling. A " Romeo and Juliet," engaged in what is meant for a parting embrace, but which looks more like the commencement of a wrestling match; a " Moonlight Scene," by hM. Auguste Rdgnier, as blue as if it had been dipped in a dyer's vat; a " Daniel in the Lions' Den," the prophet surrounded by a whole menagery of fero. ciou'lbeasts, over whose heads a detachment of angels are -floeting, waving their arms and apParently mesmerizing the animals into submission. There is "L'Enfcr," by one Chenavard, who must surely have dreamed it after a heavy supper, or he couldahardly have devised so disgusting a set of horrors. Tho " Murder of Queen Galswinthe by a slave, by order and before the eyes of her husband chilperic," is ano jl,"5pleasaiit subject, and unpleasantly treated.
Thelis Rafine gorgeGus " Spauish Bull-fight," by Porion, taken from a description by Theophile Gautier in his Tica los Miron*cs; horses and toreros roiling on the ground in a cloud of dust; chulos waving their crimson cloaks; galleries ftUl of pretty women and eager spectators; and the bright sky of Seville above all. A Florentine "Primavera," by MUller, is also much admired. The lady in the foreground, with llac in her hair, is a most lovely creature. The physiognomy of a negro with a tropical bira upon his wrist, keeping off the playfull attack of an Italian greyhound, has
character, and makes an excellent contrast-with the fair and beautiful countenance of the woman.
It is rather difficult to say what is the distinguishing feature of this year's exhibition. A great deal was expected, and I think it not improbable that the general feeling may be one of disappointment, from the 'absence of anAivin very striking, although there are many pretty pictures, some good ones, and but a moderate number of ultra-bad. A great deal of ingenuity is displayed in
some of the tc&eaux de 9e21-e, and those generally take well with the Parisian public, who love to see an idea smartly renderedby a picture, particularly if it be comic.
Of the merits of the exhibition as a whole I wi not give an opinion, for it requires more than a six hours' ramble amongst the maze of canvass to be able to do so with cor-
rectness. I am inclinod to thik, however, that the exposition of 1846 will prove at leastofavieage.merit.

EXHIBITION OF ENGLISH ARTISTS.
The plan of opening an extra exhibition in which tbe aUtists of Great Britain, having the jurisdiction 6f particular societies, may come on their own autlbority before the public, seems to have succeeeded. Last year the gallery of the old Water Colour o *iety "was opened for a collection of sketches and drawings by Engribh artists, as a novel experiment, ax.d thi year the experiment is repeated. The chief feature of the gallery is the new aspect under which some of the best modern painters are presented. The. water-colour men are, indeed, much in the same position as when they paint for their own soQieties, but many of the painters of large works in. oil come forward here, covAtiDg a smaller surfaceh Than when they paint for the Academy or tbe British lnstitution, and giving their conceptions fresh from 'he brain without undergoing the toil of elaboration. IThe best artists of the day exhibit in great numbers, but the class that is most brought forward is the historical and epic. Landseape painteis and students of dramatic life are alm ays to be found in cabinet dimenuione, but there is a novel interest in catching the first notion of an historical artist. The inspirations which Mr. Pickersgill has caught from Ariosto and Spenser, aud has reproduced now in a small picture, now in a rapid sketch, are, on this account, amon g the most striking works in the collection.

TALKING OF BOOKS
I
HYMNS
AS POETRY
By Oliver Edwards
It has long struck me as strange that the anthologists of English poetry have shown so very little appreciation of the literary merits of many of the English hymns. The Golden Treasury contains not one, and The Oxford Book of English Verse but a very few. Those which Q admitted were an early sixteenthcentury example, Henry Vaughan's " My Soul there is a Country," and Addison's " The Spacious Firmament on High." One might have thought that, after so nobly, with that last poem, ushering in the great era of English hymn-writing-let us call it that from Addison to Newmanthe anthologist would have been emboldened to give some other examples. But no. There is not another hymn (in the generally accepted sense of the word) in his selection of what was finest among the shorter English poems.
Can it be that the English have so much the habit of keeping their various types of thought and feeling in separate compartments of their minds that they cannot think of hymns as literature ? Or is it a form of Puritanism which refuses to see aesthetic qualities in an essentially religious exercise of words? If this latter be true the Puritanism is one not shared by our neighbours the Welsh, for when the late W. J. Gruffydd in 1931 compiled his Welsh anthology he drew more numerously upon William Williams Pantycelyn, the greatest of the Welsh eighteenth-century hymn-writers, than upon any other poet except Ceiriogand one of the latter's contributions, even, was a hymn. Yet the best English hymn-writers were by any unbiased standards poets of very notable rank.
The hymn, as a literary form, has one special claim upon the esteem of anyone who has been a professional writer. In the hymn the writer must express himself With sincerity of feeling, vividness of phrase, and eloquence, within a comparatively limited choice of metres, and within a range of subject-matter and thought dictated to him by the tenets of his faith. There ale, let it be admitted, a vast number of hymns in which this searching test has been too much for the poet's skill. And there are a good many cases, too, in which words and tune form so much a single unit in the mind that it is hardly possible to disentangle the strictly literary element. I remember that the singing in school chapel of " I praised the earth in beauty seen," with the moving final lines of its first stanza, "And earth and ocean seemed to say, Our beauties are but for a day," brought to the adolescent mind a sudden sharp realization of this world's transitory nature-a lasting emotional and aesthetic (though not I think religious) experience. Yet how much of that effect was due to Heber's words, and how much to the noble air by John Hullah to which we sang them, I cannot now attempt to determine.
For all that, however, there are Many hymns about whose merits as poetry there can be no doubt at all-especially among those of the eighteenth century.
There was a precision, combined sometimes with orotundity, in the poetic diction of that time which served the hymn-writer admirably, and which he could use with complete sincerity-as could be instanced again and again from Charles Wesley, and perhaps above all from Isaac Watts. How can any anthology of English poetry be adequate which does not contain the poem that has probably moved more English people more deeply than any other? "Our God, our help in ages past" (which is how Watts began his poem) is full of the most vivid and exquisitely phrased images. And in Watts, too, are some of those overwhelming anthropomorphic visions which were characteristic of his century:-
My God, who mak'st the sun to know
His proper hour to rise,
And to give light to all below,
Dost send him round the skies.
There is, implicit in that stanza, a vivid way of seeing things which one finds again, half a century or more later, in the most beautifully worded of Cowper's hymns:-
God moves in a mysterious way,
His wonders to perform:
He plants His footsteps in the sea,
And rides upon the storm.
Try mentally to materialize those two stanzas, and is the result not something very close in kind to the visions which William Blake expressed in watercolour ?
Nor are these isolated, or especially rare, instances. The eighteenth century supplies many other hymns which may be read with aesthetic enjoyment; and in one of these, Toplady's " Rock of Ages," there is an impassioned mystic self-abasement, almost a self-torture, very different from the common placid standard of religious feeling anid expression in England:-
Nothing in my hand I bring; Simply to Thy Cross I cling;
Naked, come to Thee for dress;
Helpless, look to Thee for Grace; Foul, I to the fountain fly;
Wash me, Saviour! or I die.
How can the anthologist - unless blinded by some irrelevant consideration -have failed to appreciate the qualities of such poetry ? It is too much to believe him so inhuman that he can have read, without trembling on the verge of tears, H. F. Lyte's sonorous, melancholy, and yet uplifting valediction:
Swift to its close ebbs out life's little day; Earth's joys grow dim, its glories pass away; Change and decay in all around I see;
0 thou who changest not, abide with me.
Rather one imagines that some stubborn, half- unconscious resistance, based on the feeling that poetry ought, at least in itssimplerforms, to beabletomake its effects without calling in aid all the spiritual suggestion which lies within the religious and devotional themej that it should bea "pure" art, has as a rule inhibited him from giving way to his own instinctive feeling. If so, it is time that he should recant, perhaps taking the very words of his recantation from another great and beautiful hymn:
"Pride ruled my will: remember not
p-ast years."

Autobiography is an area in which writers tend to be self-centred . Alan Ross is the exception to the rule, writes Chris Peachment A lan Ross wants his two volumes of autobio - graphy , Blindfold Games and Coastwise Lights (the latter pub - lished this week) to be seen as the raw material from which he began to write poetry, and only incident - ally autobiographical. He is being unduly modest, because they are more than the prosaic pretext for the poems which stud the books. This second volume is also in part a fascinating history of metropolitan literary life from the end of the war, when Ross was demobbed from the navy (Able Seaman, Russian Con - voys ), through the Soho and Fitzrovia set of the late Forties, past the more recherche figures of
the Fifties, such as Julian Mac - laren - Ross and Henry Green, and up to the Sixties, when he edited the London Magazine. It is also a collection of character sketches of people he has known; he writes most tenderly about painters such as John Minton and Keith Vaughan. So where, to echo Roy Campbell, is the bloody horse? The answer to that is: in every single well-chosen word. Ross might not be elbowing his way to the foreground as is the practice in much modern autobiography, but nevertheless a picture emerges of a perceptive man, with a rare liking for his fellow men, a strong interest in cricket and horses, and a fine prose style. One wonders why he had not been a novelist.
"I did try once," he says, "but without much conviction. It al - ways seems extraordinary to me how, out of all the multifarious things that happens to you, a no v - elist can settle on one subject and turn it into a book. The war was tremendous for someone like me; it gave you a subject gift-wrapped ."
His shipboard adventures, in - cluding being sealed alone in the forward magazine of a sinking ship with a hose pipe to play on the glowing metal, are expertly told in the first volume, Blindfold Games. But while at sea, "all I wanted to do when I got ashore again was to head for Soho. The grand life, that others.dreamed of, never appealed." He maintains that Soho was at its greatest just before the end of the war, after which most of the people went off to find jobs, leav - ing only the most fuddled of hangers-on still cadging drinks at the bar. Julian Maclaren-Ross , who was the model for the exper - imental novelist X. Trapnel in Anthony Powell's Dance to the Music of Time sequence, was an enemy of promise. "He should have been tremendously success - ful . He was very gifted. He had an enormous memory for the di - alogue of all the films he had seen, and would have made a good scriptwriter. But everything in
him was failure. He would take 10 advances from different publish - ers , and therefore be unable to publish with any of them, because the other nine would be wanting their money back. His fur coat and his cane, all that/o//e de grandeur would have been fine if he had found someone to look after him, and give him a sense of worth." R oss was more fond of Henry Green, whose strange gerund-titled novels {Living, Loving, Doting , Ending ) go in and out of fashion. "His father ran a small industrial firm, and for most of his life Henry made fun of him shuffling about the office moving a piece of paper from desk to desk. But then when Henry inherited the firm, he just couldn't make a go of it, and drank himself to death in no time. He had been such a handsome, dandyish man, but by the end he looked like a tramp. It was very sad." Ross had long been friendly with painters and illustrators, and
the sale of some carefully bought works of art enabled him to buy a race horse. "In 20 years, I won about 30 races so it just about paid its way and was great fun. It cost about £5,000 a year to stable and train a horse, so if you win a couple of races then it will pay its way. Nowadays though, there are very few small British owners left. It's .the sort of hobby that was possible then, but no longer." His other great passion was cricket, and if a man could write that England never lost a Test series throughout his career as the Observer 's cricket correspondent then he can justly boast of having lived through a Golden Age. Nowadays, he devotes himself solely to editing the London Magazine, which he does single - handed from a shed in the back garden in South Kensington. It has an enviable record of introducing new writers to the public, and he can claim William Boyd and Graham Swift, among others, as
recent proteges
The life of small magazines, how - ever , does not become easier with the passing of time : "The Arts Council has been very generous in the past, but 1 suspect that every - thing soon will have to be self - sup - porting . When I began in 1960 a phone call to Chatto or Faber or OUP would guarantee an advert - isement being placed simply be - cause they thought it was a respon - sible thing to do. Nowadays, some accountant bleats something about "cost-effectiveness" and so they don't do it. Now people are talking about sponsorship, but sponsors always want a box at the theatre or a seat at Lords. Not much mileage in a literary magazine." It is iniquitous that one has to keep reminding people, in these Thatcherite times, that there are some activities which should never be judged by the size of their wallet. "Oh there are lots of things," says Ross, "that really ought to lose money." Coastwise Lights by Alan Ross, pub - lished this week by Collins (£11.95)

SALOMONS CONCERT.
Mr. Salomon held his first Subscription Concert on Monday night, which was a most exquisite treat to the Amateurs of real fine Music. The Vocal Selection consisted of two" Songs by Viganoni, two by Braham, and two by Mara, with a Duetto’ by her and Viganoni, in which these eminent Singers outvied each,other "by their different excellencies. The Instrumental part consisted of two grand Symphonies, composed by Haydn, which were performed in a stile that enraptured, the Audience. The two Messrs. Morals, lately arrived from Bavaria, played a Concertante for two violins, with a brilliancy and neatness of execution, seldom equalled; anti a Mr. Himmel, Mailer of the Chapel to the King of Pruffia, who also lately came to this Country, treated the Audience with a Sestetto, in the composition os which he not only displayed science, blended with taste and feeling, but also the most uncommon powers of execution and effect on the piano forte.
The Company was highly respectable. His Royal Highness the Prince of Wales and the Duke of Cambridge, besides several of the fit st Nobility ami Gentry being present; but it was,not st. numerous as the entertainment deserved, owing partly, as we understand, to the Private Theatricals in Tottenbamcourt-road, having been postponed from the preceding Monday, and happening consequently t<> chilli with Mr. Salomon’s first night. It is to be hoped, however, that the real taste and liberality of those who have the means of encouraging such elegant entertainments, will be sufficient amply to repay the Conductor of them for his trouble and expences.

AlbitEtsR CONCERT.
The second Subscription. Confcert Supper and Ball took place last Monday evening, at the New Rooms, Hanover-square. Though the Concert commenced about eight o’clock, the Room was not completely filled till it was nearly ten, at which hour his Royal Highness the Prince of Wales graced the assembly with his presence, but retired soon after the Concert was concluded. The Orchestra, as on the former occasion, was partly composed of amateurs, and partly of professional performers. The principal instrumental' performers of the latter description were Miss Castelo, who played the harp; Mr. Holmes, the bassoon'; aud Master Morri, the violin: the singers were, Mrs, Bianchi, Mess. Gore, Evans, Nield, Vaughan, Masters Kellner, Guttler, &c. The Concerto-violin, by Master Morri, a little boy, between seven and eight years of age, called forth the approbation and astonishment of every person present. A song, beautifully composed by the Honourable J. Spencer, was sung by Master Kellner. The selection of music for this occasion consisted of favourite pieces from the works of Handel, Dussek, Yanovick, Haydx, Harrison, &c. the whole of which were most favourably received by the company. The Concert ended between eleven and twelve, when the company retired to partake of an elegant supper prepared for them in the apartment below ; which not being large enough to contain above one half of the Subscribers, the glees were performed in the Concert Room. After five or six glees by the singers above named, and ably accompanied on the harpsichord by Mr. Spencer, the ball commenced in the same room, which had been cleared for the purpose. From the attention and exertions of Mr. Second, dancing-master, as Master of the Ceremonies, the greatest regularity prevailed amongst the dancers, whom we left at this amusement.—Besides his Royal Highness the Prince of Wales, we perceived the following Nobility and Gentry amongst the company:—
D«cAi*.qes-yDevoiishire, Marlborough, Bolton, and Leeds.
Countesses—Dartmouth, Buckinghamshire, Kenmare, Stanhope, Westmeath, and Carhampton.
The Margravine of Anspach.
Ladies—Melbourne, Melville, Heathcote, Elizabeth Monk, Percival, Jane James.
Mrs. Fitzberbert.
Lufts—Stanhope, Westmeath, and Fortescue.
Lords—Chief Baron, Beresford, and Macdonald.
Sirs—Allan Gardner, Walter James, George Beaumont, and George Shee. Dr. Burney.

Sample 48:

CONVERT AT THE MANON-HOUE
The Concert at the Mansion-house last night was crowded beyond all precedent. The Egyptian-hall, beautifully illuminated with gas-light, was filled long before the commencement of the performance, and at eight o’clock not a single seat was to be procured tor the numerous and elegantly dressed women who continued to arrive in successive parties for a considerable time afterwards. 'The performances were of a very high order, and afforded a combination of vocal and instrumental harmony seldom surpassed in the estimation of the musical world. We have not time to particularise individual merits : it gives us pleasure, however, to testify to the arduous and successful exertions of all those eminent performers who so handsomely rendered their services on this occasion. The offer was the more liberal, inasmuch as the object was one which could only interest those who feel an anxious desire for improving the situation of the lower orders, and had no connexion with any subject of a political or national character. Our readers are probably aware, that the profits of the exhibition are intended to be’applied to the purpose of extricating the Royal Institution for the Education of Poor children from its present pecuniary embarrassments. The practical utility of such an object appears to us as indisputable, as the benevolence which suggested and is still active inromotinit.

CONCERT AT THE EGYPTIAN HAZE-
. A.eoncert was held yesterday eveningat the Egyptian-hall, in? the Mansion-house, under the patronage of her most gratiousSlajesty Queen ^Caroline, for the benevolent purpose ot relieving from an oppressive debt the. Soya! Institution for the - education,, on the British system, of 1,000 boys and SOfrgtrls, of all religious denominations, in two capacious schools situate in North-street, City-road. It had been well known'for many days before this concert took place,that her Majqsty. intended to honour it with her presence; and, in consequence, no inconsiderable crowd was last night arectribled to greet her coming with their'unbought acclamations. From 5 o’dockm the afternoon ttllft o’clock in the evening die anxiety Of tho people to receive her Majesty with that affectionate loyalty sehttit they always seel towards her was apparent in all quarters, but mpst-particularly in Cheapside and the adjacent streets/rhere can be Ijftle doubt in the minds of thoro who paid attention to the discourse Of the multitude which was yesterday evening collected in that part Ot-the town, font, if her Majesty had been desirous of,enjoyuig another popular triumph, she might then have enjoyed it to the very summit of her wish, as every mouth was filled with praise of her undaunted courage, and her kind and generous behaviour to all who have the happiness of being in any way connected with her. But her Majesty, who seeks not popularity by her conduct, but who, on the contrary, is sought for by it, disappointed the expectations of her many friends and admirers, and instead of ostentatiously attempting to catch their applause, modestly withdrew from it by directing her course to the Mansion-house through the by streets of the metropolis instead of through its great and public thoroughfares, foured it would have been almost impossible for her to have reaches the Mansion-house by the way of Cheapsidcr without creating trie very greatest contusion in it, as its whole line from Si. Paul s church-yard tn. the Mansion-house was filled from half past five o clock till seven with an unbroken chain of carriages. The doors of the EgrotnmTidll were opened to the numerous comers by six o clock, and before many minutes had elapsed from the opening, the hall was crowded to repletion. Her Majesty, though she left Brandenburgh-house at half past five, did not enter the haU till nearly seven. We cannot conclude our account of this concert without congratulating rhe friends of the excellent charity which it was intended to /support on the success which attended their efforts on the present occasion* The scats in the centre of the Hall are calculated to accommodate 18C0 persona : they were completely filled, as were also the .'benches az id niches placed on the aides of it. Indeed so full was every particle of ground which could be occupied, that we are sure tnat there could not be less than 2,300 persons present; and we are told that the net proceeds of this concert to the charity trill amount to no less a sum than 1000 guineas. It would also be improper in us to take our leave of it without remarking that the arrangements of it were exceedingly well go tup, and that we have never seen assembled in one place a greater portion of beauty and respectability.
Her Majesty on leaving the Mansion-house was loudly greeted "by the populacd, and her carriage drove off at half past 10 o’clock, amidst the loudest acclamations of the surrounding multitudes.

MR. MOSCHELRS' CONCERT. I
±'sr. issOscMe1es gave his armnual concert last night at the Argyll-rooms, the spacious area of whioh was crowded to overflov with fashionable company; musical connoisseurs, and tle ead' be of the profession. As a or. er_
the pian onrte, MTr. Aloscheles must be placed In.the-verySrrst rank of his art; and if we consider the variety of power he displays, thbre are few names indeed, which deserve to stand on the same level with his. In the neatnesR and rapiditv of his execution he leaves nothing to be desired, and combines with those qualities a graceful elegance of expression which was considered a novelty in the art till others acquired it by imitating himn, and by the practic of his compositions. As a composer, too, be must be allowed to possess great talent, and his management of the fanciful embroiderv of a simple sub cot, without destroying the mdlody, forms in him a pa,cuuar merit. His first piece in the performances of last night was a MIS. concerto, by Hummel, played for the first time in this country. As a compositlon, wit] the exception of the slow movement, it was below mediocrity, an the accompaniments, in the true German style, too often overpowered and destroyed the brilliancy of the pincipal instrtument. In the second act of the concert mr. Moseheles, however,gave a piece in which his full talent both as a composer and performer was displayed. le designates it his ' Recollections of Ireland; a fartasiafor the piano-forte, with orchestral accompaniments." The plan of the composition, if it may be said to have a plan, is wild and irneg-ar. Like the bee roving from flower to flower, he snatcheg in succession well-known beauiiful national airs of the sister kingdom, and clothes them, without destroying their beauty, in the richest yet most c aracteristic decoration. Each passage confessed the band of the master, and the performance, which. was just of the proper length to keep the attention unimpaired was honoured at its conclusion with the most livdy appiause. But MIr. Moscheles did not, as other great protessors have done, trust to himself for the sole attraction. 1. Von Weber, who presided together with Sir ff. Smart, conducted the orchestra in several of his own compositions, consistng of the noble overture to his opera of Euruanthe, equallysuiking and original, thoughless known than the celebrated overture to Frreschutz; an air, with recitative, channingly sung, by Madame Caradori; and a

CONCEkRT AT GUILDHALL.
On Saturdaymoymin n ucert took place at Guildhall, for the benefit of the distressed weavers atSpitalfields and Bethnal-green. The performance consisted of the Oratorio of the Messiah. WVe were at first inclined to think that the seleetion of this composition was somewhat injudicious, the object being to excite general interest rather than to gratif, the select few; but the intense attention with which the perform ance was listened to by a numerous and rather mixed anudience proved that we were-mistaken, and afferded convincing evidence that Handel wrote not only "for all -fine," but for all tastes. .-Though the masterpieceof thegreat master is-so familiar as to bekmown almost by heart by all who bestow attention: to music, yet tbh splendid and complete manner intwhich it -was heard on Saturday, imparted to it an air of novelty. It was performed, as we believe it now invariably .is, writh the addition of Mozart's accompaniments, which were composed at the request of Baron Von Swieten, of Vienna, with the view of bringing the work as near as possible to a level with the great progress in instrumce tal music. These additions, albeit profane in the estimia. tioa of the exclusive admirers of what has laeen flippantly denominated the "yty-wig school " cannot fail to gratify ears accustomed to the rich orchestral combinations of more recent composers. Mozart's addidons to the Messiah are chiefly confined to the airs, and they consist for the most part of the introduc.o, of harmonies for the wind instruments, which in Handel's time were but sparingly employed. The numerous orchestra at Guildhall rendered the beauty of Mlozart's decorations strikingly manifest. The performers, both vocal 3iid instrumental; contributed their exertions gratuitously. Nor was WViUman's accompaniments on the corno bassetto at all inferior to the singing. Signor Costa does not rise in our opinion. His intonation was again very imperfect; and, common as this fault is in the vocalists of Italy, there is no excellence whichcan compensate for it.
The performance of The Messiah this mornin- necessarily abridges our notice of this day's proceedings. it is played so often by the same band and sung by the same singers, that it goes like a piece of well-constructed machinery. We anticipate every note and every effect. The chief novelty--and that is only a novelty here-was the addition of AMozart's accompaniments. It is rather strange that a festival which has been used to lead the public taste should be, in this case, the last to follow it, and that in a point on which all first-rate musicians have long been agreed. We cannot help noticing an instance of wretclhed taste in the allotment of the air, " Be shall feed his flock," to Mliss F. CITY CONCERT.
The first of the City Amateur Concerts for the season took place last night its the great roeom of the London Tavern, under the directon of the saine gentlemen who conducted them on previous occasions. NORWICH MUSICAL FESTIVAL.
- Friday, Sept. 24.
The concerts of last night opened rith the Jupier Sin.
fonfa of Dlozart, to wbich the band did ample ju.stice. It is unnecessary to repeat our commendations of the songs which Madame Il1alibran selected for thlis evening's performance; In Willman's accompaniment of her ANon pin di faoi, it seemed as if he had caught fresh inspiration from her singing. The finale to Fidelio, by Beethoven, dosed the first act. This gigantic production is kindred to theconnlusion of his sinfonia inC sminor: the subjectispresented with such imposing power, and renewedand multiplied with such consummate sk-ill. that it may be classed among the greatest efforts of the author's genius. The selection for this morning began with "1The Dedication of the Temple," by 3slozact. The impressive solo, "Great Israel's God," was well sung. But the great feature of the performance vwas the new oratorio " The last Judgment," by Spohr, a work combining more true genius with riginality than we ever remember to bave heard. Spohr is trained in the school of Beethoven and WeYberx and this composition partakes of what somie would term ist extrava-
ancies ; but it is quite of a new character, and must net be measured by the grave simplicity of Handel, nor the more florid style of Haydn. The subject is one which only a real genius would venture to aPproach, and which does, in fact, allow, and even demand, those extraordinary combinations of which the severer writers of times past would never dream. Our space will not admit a detailed analysis of this extraordinary work, and we maust content ourselves with noticing the solo, in which the approach of "the great day" is announced- the sublime ehorus, beginning with the words "tDestroycd is Babylon the mighty !" and the exquisite quartett and semi-chorus "Blest are the departed." Thieeffectof this scene ismagnificent beyond description, and the extraordinary correctness and point with which it was performed by this mighty band were above all praise. The festival closed with a selection from Handel's " Israel in Egypt," and never was the simple grandeur of his double chorusses more poverfully and accurately developed. The answering choirs were well balanced, and the responding phrases produced, with the support of so large a number of voices, a maagnificent effect.
The hall this morning, as well as last night, was crowded. Among the company we observed the Earls of A]bemarlc and Orford, Lord Suffield Lord Cailtlorpe Sir IV. Folkes, DI.P., Robert Grant, Esq.; 3.IIL, Sir SY. &;euchamp, and must of the resident nobility and gentry. A large namber of strangers also, from most parts of the kingdom, were attracted by the high reputAtion of the Norwich I cstival. Dlr. Cokke was absent. Ve believe it is an understood thing in Norfolk that where (as in the present case) a member of the lVodehouse family stands at the head of any public institution, there Mlr. Coke's support is not to be reckoned upon.
The festival is one of the public efforts of the county of Norfolk of which it may well be proud. Its band is secocad in numerical strength, but second only, to York, and in discipline very far its superlor. The sacred selections at York are deformed by the insertion of very second-rate and trifling compositions. At Norwich they are entirely free from every blemish of this kind; and whether we regard the music chosen, the magnificent effect of the band, the admirable regularity which pervades every department, and the consummate skill with which the whole was corntrolled and guided by the conductor, the Festival of this city stands first in excellence.

P.AGANmNPS fONG-ERT.
Paganlni's performance on the violin Is a novelty In rt so rare and curioes, that it is Well worth recurring to. It may be maid to form a new era in the history of mutic, and to have so fir extended its bounds as to amount to a species of revolution in the most cechanting of the sciences. The violin in his hands is a newvlnstrument; its original powers and supposed extent, even as displayed by the most eminent proressors, are more than doubl ed under the toueh of Paganini. What resources, what infinite combinations, will le presented to the Mozarts and Beethovens of a future age, hobuld the violin ever be cultivated generally to any thing like the stonishing degree that It has been by him.
Psaganiniplays only music of bis own coniposition, but tbis is matter of necessity. No composper could bave produced Tnusic, the execution of which, til Paganini appeared, would have been pronounced impossible. Bis works are, however, distinguished, not.merely by mecbanical dililcultles, but rank very high as corposition. Many of them are published, and arc well known, the existence of them at least, to musicians. They have passed; however, except by those who have heard him slay, as a delusion,-an utter impracticability. The most skilful violinists have declared, that so far from being able to execute them, they could not even Imagine how they could be played. We allude more particularly to bis capriclos, which are in the possession of every professor of the lnstrumtnt, and must have produced despair in the breast of many an aspiring student, at beilng baffled in all the e*orts to sur. mount their inconceivable diffculdties
It is hlghly necessary, we conceive, to do away with one PIcc of injusice which it is very common to hear committed a instPaganini. There is not the least degree, not a tittle oibarlatanism or trick about him. By the term " trick" in vlin playing is meant something which appears difficult, b which ceases to be so when the mode of producing the e ct Is explained. Those who decry Paganini on thbs g und-for we regret to say tkere is more envy and malice to be met with In the science of harmony than in any ofher,would not, perhaps, fiad those difficulties much lessened If they were placed as pupils under the professor himself. The " tricks" of- Papanini are all at essential part of the splendid effects he briags forth. No true muican would part with one of them. But even uncultivated ears, we suspect, will find a grcat charm In Pags. nini: he cannotbe appreciated so weS bIttitIsagreat erfir to imagine that he can be appreciated by musicians alone. All his difficulties are beauties, and beautif4l often in propor. tion to the difficulty. Dr. Johnson once said, when pestered with remarks about the difficulty of a violin concerto. that he wished it had been impossible. This applies justly eiough to most of the mere fiddling passages our ears have been traiaed to endure in public, but the unmusical lexicographer himself would neyer have thought of applying the observation to Pag anii.
It seems almost hyperbolical to say It, but the fact is so, th at Pagauini's playing isin a very high degree intellectual. It Is mental as well as physical, and mechanical. The Instant lie sels hisvioltn, which h6usually coquetawithfor a timebefore bringing it up to its proper place, a sudden animation pase
over his countenance, before cold and inexpressive. He has the advantage, which all concerto players by the way ought to adopt, of never nsing a book. Thismode in Itselfhs
much the superiority as a speech deiivertd has over one that is read. When the first bow Js drawn, Paganinl Is evidently lost to every other thought, and is revelliag probably in a world cf his own c reation. Al his passages seem fiee and unpremeditated, as if conceived on the Instant. One his no impression of their having cost him either forethought or la-
our The word difficulty has no place In his vocabulary, so completely is all brought under his subjection and mastery.
Nothing can Ibe mote intense in feeling than his conception and delivery of an adagio passage. His tone Is not, perhaps, so faU and round as that of some other players-ss Ballot or De -Beriot, for example: It Is delicate, rather than strong; but that delicacy is inconcelvable unless one has heard it, and was probably never possessed equaBy by any other player. His touch is occasionally so fine, that the note seems to float In the air, and not to spring from any instrunent. In point of expression, it is Impossible to imagine any thingmoreperfect. Themelancholy,ortender, asshould be the case in slow movements, mostly predorminate, but there is no shade or form of expression which the genius of Pags. alni does not draw forlt. His adagios are intermixed wIth passages of rspid execution wbich go off with the rapidity of
rocket or a flling star-a, break of the subject, or an inpertinence in any handabut his own-but If antlysed, all is in perftet keeping.
The only thing tha,t can be aid to lessen the wonder of Pganini's powers in the way of mere mechanism is, hat he is indebted for them in sommeasure to his own peeuliar conformation. His long arms and slender frame allow bimr to place the instrument in the most advantageous poition that is possible, and his left arm Is brought so completely under It, tbat his hand seems to cover the whole extent of the finger-board. Such Is the tlexibilitr, besides, of bis joints, that he can throw his thumb nearly back upon bis wrist, and extendI bis little finger, at the same time, in the opposite direction. By these mneans, when In the first position, as it is called, of the violin, he can reacb, without shifting, to the second octave. His extreme high notes,-for he contrives to play tbreeoctrves on each string,-
e en consequently, with a precision and certainty never hesrd=betore. ITnisflexibility, without doubt, is indispensable to the execution of many of the passages, though It Is, proba bly, not wholly Batural to him but acquired, in part, by 'his long and severe practice. hi solo on the fourtb, or 'rf string, the other three being discarned for the occa-
won, we consider among the mnost charming, as well as 'the most wonderful, specimens. For the sake of musicians who have nelther heard PaRanini nor seen his compositions, we notice a few of those ef. i`ccs, which are wholly, or for the most part, peculiar to himself.
1. A succession of chords, in thirds, sixths, and octaves, in plain rythm, and In semi-tones, in the quickest measuie, over the whole extent of the Instrument.
2. A series of shakes on two notes at the same time, thirds, sixths, or octaves. The latter is imnpractlfeble perbaps but with a hand as flexible as that of Paganini. He also makes a double shake on the same note, but produced on different stringe.
3. A complete scale In harmonic notes, which he forms double, and even executes double shakes in them. We be. lieve there are veryfew,even among musicians,who understand the mode by which this peculiar enfect is produced.
4. A continued shake on one note, with wbich he unites chromatic passages over the whole exteat of the instrumnent.
5. His combination of the legato or bowed notes, with the nizzicato, or notes produced by thefingers only. Vith tkese ieforms an accompanimentin doubleortreble notes,sustaining
rich and full melody the while with the bow. He sometimes aries the form of the pizxicato, by passages altemately pro. laced by the finger and tbe bow, and by making sbakes with he finger alone, unassisted by the bow.
Tbese, perhaps, will suffice for this purpose ; It would be !ndless to enter into all the minutlte of Paganini's skill. To udge of him properly, he must be heatd. mong more books for boys we have Old Gold, or the Cruise or the Jason (Nister), in which Mr. Manville Fenn is at his best. An exploring party goes up the Amazon, and several of the characters are capital—the skipper, his mates, and the sundried American who has pre-eminently the push and pluck of his compatriots. The story is packed full of startling incidents ; the Indians make things unpleasantly warm, and perhaps there is an improbable superabundance of sensational experiences in the zoological department. In Far Bolivia, by Dr. Gordon Stables (Blackie), takes us once again to the head waters of the mighty Amazon. We like it better than most of Dr. Stables’s stories, because he is less Scottish and more cosmopolitan than usual. There is a fair plot with picturesque sketches of tropical scenes. Allan Adair (R. T. 8.), is by the same writer ; Allan, as usual, being a Scot, and taking his departure from Tayside. He sees the world with a vengeance, circumnavigating the globe, and having a succession of
miraculous escapes from death in all conceivable forms. Won from the Waves (Griffith, Farran) is by the late Mr. Kingston, but we have not met with it before, nor are we told that it is a new edition. Anyhow it is a capital story, breezy with the breath of the ocean wave, and full of fighting at sea and on shore. The scenes are laid in the period when Earl Howe was the hero of the day, and with heroes of all ranks there are villains galore. Besides there are two pretty love stories, where the lovers are tossed on the billows of fate till drifted into port. In In the King’s Service, by Captain Brereton (Blackie), are chapters ot sensational Irish history, describing the storming of Drogheda and Cromwell’s thoroughgoing methods from the point of view of a young Royalist who pushed the Protector’s lieutenants hard. A Trek and a Laager and Early Days Among the Boers are two stories of
South African adventure, in periods long before the latest trouble with the Boers. The former, by Jane Speltague (Blackie), narrates the fortunes of a venturesome pioneer family, who fell upon evil times between wild beasts and ferocious Kaffirs. It is pleasant, lively, and instructive. “ Early Days” (Griffith, Farran) is by the veteran General Drayson, an authority on South African matters. It takes us back to the time when Cetewayo was beginning to makehimself obnoxious; whereas Mr. Johnston's One of Buller’s Horse (Nelson) brings us down to the campaign when Cetewayo tempted Providence and won a victory only to be crushed. For God and the King (Bousfleld) is a good collection of “ True Stories of Heroic Women,” by Sir Edward Creasy, Lady Fanshawe, and other writers. They go back to the days of the Heptarchy, and the more modern heroines range from Jeanne d’Arc to the Maid of Saragossa. A very pretty little volume is Dickens’s Holly Thee and Seven Poor Travellers (Dent), with photogravures and a profusion of clever and comical drawings by C. E. Broek, illustrating habits and costumes lonout of dat

Sample 102:
==================================================

Dr. Dugald Mitchell’s object in writing a History oe the Highlands (Alexander Gardner) has been to provide a history “ of moderate length.” A public debauched by primers and manuals will regard a book of about 700 closely printed pages as immoderately long. Highland history is very complex, and as up to the 16th century its records were mainly to be found in oral tradition, or in ill-informed Lowland authors, Highland history is very obscure. Dr. Mitchell is copious, industrious, conscientious, and, to an almost startling degree, impartial. We have compared his book with many other records of the same events, and can pronounce the work interesting and useful, though tocking in condensation and devoid of charm of style. The book should be in every Highland lodge, not that sportsmen read history, but because it- might often be found useful for references to the local affairs of the strath or glen.

NEW FRENCH BOOKS.
(FROM OUR PARIS CORRESPONDENT.)
I would once more call the attention of English readers of French books to a few of tee more remarkable of recent publications. M. Maurice Fannon, whose unlucky accident in the final day of tee Paris-Berlin nice deprived him, perhaps, of occupying the second place in teismateh, has just proved in a singularly fascinating volume,entitled “ Troig mills Kilometres en Ballon ” (Signol), that he is as daring and experienced an explorer of tee air as ho is a master automobilist. The illustrations which accompany this volume are often from photographs of tee earth’s surface as seen from tho balloon and are curiously interesting. There are seven chapters dealing with
as many picturesque journeys in mid-air, during which physiographic observations of every sort were taken. The layman will be less amused by M. Maurice Gaget’s remarkable monograph on “ La Navigation Sous-marino ” (Beranger), but he will do well to take up the book if it be only to peruse the last chapters, in which, after" technical explanations of the various types of submarine vessels, M. Gaget discusses how far tee theories of modern naval warfare will have to be reconsidered by our naval strategists owing to the r6le ot tee submarine vessel. I cannot too much insist on tee Buggestiveness for naval specialists of tee chapter on “ Privateering and grande guerre,” or of tho last two, in which tee problem of a FrancoEnglish war is treated from tho *point of view of the efficacy and utility of the submarine vessel. In another order of ideas let me note the appearance of a curious journal in two volumes, written from 1775 to 1780 by the French Charge d’Affaires in Russia, M. de Corberon, " Un Diplomats Franqais 1 la Cour de Catherine H.” (Pion). Tho picture of Catherine’s Court as given hers is remarkably detailed and frequently piquant. A valuable introduction of nearly 75 pages is contributed by M. Labaude. Lovers of Balzac have a treat before them in a book published by Ollendorff—“La Genese d’un Romon, de Balzac.” This is one of those characteristic volumes by the distinguished Belgian collector and scholar, tee Vicomte de Spoelberch de Lovenjoul. The novel in question is " Les Paysans.” Nothing in their kind could be more curious than the revelations here made of how Balzac conceived his stories, of his manner of working them out, and of his general artistic temperament'. There are here, apart from a sheaf of fresh letters, some score of unpubliteed pages of Balzac, all ingeniously utilized by this erudite Maecenas, M. de Lovenjoul, to whom French literature owes some of the most curious bibliographic discoveries of our time. It is not likely that the authors of the three current novels that also lie on my table—“ Le Joyau de la Mitre” (Ollendorff), " Une Ame Obscure” (Ollendorff), and “ Fiancde d’Outre Mer ” (Lemerre)—will ever receive the honours which some of their predecessors periodically receive at the hands of the Belgian scholar; but one of them, at least—the translator of
Byron, Mme. Daniel Lesucur—is not unworthy of the widest recognition, for she is to-day at the head of women writers in Franco, and all three books are to be recommended to English readers in search of recent French fiction. Everything relating to Napoleon is now interesting, and M. Monee Grasilier, who is an adept in exploring the national archives, has published in the Revue .Retrospective and reprinted as a pamphlet the official documents on the kidnapping of Sir George Rumbold at Hamburg in 1801. They give a curious account of Rumbold’s capture and his shipment from Cherbourg to England on a promise never again to approach French ports.

NEW ERENCB BOOKS.

PARIS, Nov. 11.
I have omitted of late to notice the “ novelties ” of tiie French publishing world, but this omission wm not due to any want of matter. New boohs accumulate at this season like mushrooms, and among them one might without difficulty bury oneself away from the world. All that one can do is to take a small group at hazard from the pile, and this is what I now venture to do.
First, as to the novels. The Paris of the boulevards has been talking much of late about a curious tale entitled “ La Giaffe ” (Flammarion), which M. Pierre de Lano has written—in a spirit of La Bruycre, but, I hasten to add, not with his capacity. This story has aroused excitement not because it is distinguished by any special literary merit, but because it shows us, behind the thinnest of veils, the figure of a certain ex-President of tho Republic, whose propos another author had rendered entertaining. I
_ would not pretend that the incidents herein related are such as the historian of the Third Republic can afford to utilize without careful sifting, hut there is a general truth to fact from which the cautious reader can without much difficulty construct an historical picture.
lathe “Robinsons de Faris” (Ollendorff), there is, on the other hand, no need of a key. The novel may serve as the life history of any number of the provincial minages which come to Paris fall of illusions, and, after fluttering like moths round a candle, and endeavouring to make a brave show and to live snobbishly like their neighbours, come absurdly to grief because of the silly falsehood of an ideal which would not allow them to vegetate calmly in the provinces. The book shows real ability.
j hasten to -use the same expression with regard to Mme. Jean Beitheroy’s " Mirage ” (Ollendorff), a work of great literary merit by the author of the " Dxnseuse Pompfiii,” one of the most cultivated novelists in France to-day. She here deals frankly with the problem of modem marriage—that perpetual question of the education of thejeune file which is becoming in France to-day perhaps the most presting of all. or bor nature involves a revolutionary movement through-

And finally among the novels let me mention the new story by M. Georges Ohnet, “ Le Crepuscule ” (OBendorff). M. Ohnet is holding his own, still enriching .. ' « . ...
publishers and himself, And still entertaining with, his mastery of rapid and sensational incident the
same host of readers everywhere. His clientele, however, will, I should fancy, be a little disconcerted by his latest venture. M. Ohnet would seem to be replying in this book to those of his critics who pretend that he amuses and entertains the petite bourgeoisie without awakening any thought. In “ Le Crepuscule ” the reader has a novel with an object. M. Ohnet has evidently been meditating deeply. He, too, aims here at being taken as a psychologist, treating of the law of human decadence and disintegration with a stronuousness from which, after all, the interest of his tale suffers but little. This, then,
is a new departure; but a new Ohnet is always a new Ohnet, and a multitude of readers everywhere who know the true Ohnet flavour will never lose their appetite, I will now only mention rapidly the appearance of four other volumes, each important of its kind. There is first the volume entitled “ Tout Yeux, Tout Oreilles,’’ by M. Jules Hurst (Revue Blanche), in which that brilliant journalist has collected some of his more recent interviews, giving to the whole a title which ingeniously
describes the spirit of these reflections on the Parisian theatrical and literary world. The articles published in the Temps by M. Gaston Dounet, during the Chinese imbroglio, to which their author did well to give permanent form, have also appeared as a volume with the title “ En Chine, 1900-1901 ” (Ollendorff). Finally, besides a new volume of the " Reiset Souvenirs ” (Pion), we have from Hachette a reprint of the remarkable essay on Taine by M. Giraud—the most substantial work of criticism of which Taine has ever been the object. Here is a handful of new books, taken at random from the pile which lies upon my table.—Oar Own Correspondent.
