NEW FRENCH BOOKS.
PARIS, Oct. 8.
“No man can be called happy till he is dead ” ; but even after centuries the glory assigned by posterity to the dead, and the place occupied by them in history, may be disputed. This would seem to be the lot of Columbus, if his fame were not such as to defy attacks. Mr. Henry Vignaud, the American Charge d’Affaires here, an erudite and conscientious student, whose investigations have often lit up dark comers of history, has just had published by Ernest Leroux a very interesting and, I should add, very strange volume, were it not based on the most painstaking and, in the author’s view, conclusive researches. Columbus’s son, as is well known, undertook the task of proclaiming and establishing his father’s renown, and he included in his evidence a letter from the Florentine astronomer ToscaneUi to his father encouraging him in his enterprise and lending his influence to it. Some months ago a statue of ToscaneUi was erected, and among the facts adduced for reviving his almost forgotten reputation was the writing of this letter, which was said to render him an associate in the discovery of America. Mr. Vignaud, as an ardent antiquary, set himself to investigate the relations between ToscaneUi and Columbus, and after long and persevering research he was amazed to find that they never knew each other, so that such a letter could never have passed between them. He saw that it was a forgery, and asked himself for what purpose it had been fabricated. He discovered that, after the death of Columbus, there was much talk of a pilot whose name was unknown, who, going to India, had been led into a false track and had then sailed towards America. Later, he turned back, reached India • by the regular route, and on his return told Columbus of his having seen a new continent. Columbus listened attentively to his story, and, on reflection, came to the conclusion that the pilot had found the unknown continent which had long been supposed to exist. Mr. Vignaud has convinced himself that the forged letter was designed to destroy the legend of the pilot, and to hold up Columbus as the real and sole discoverer of America. His book, written in a brilliant style and backed by a profusion of documents, tends, therefore, to prove that the glory of the discovery appertains to the unknown pilot—though doubtless to Columbus in a less degree as the providential instrument for adding the great American continent to the conquests of the Old World. Considering the arduous labours which have led Mr. Vignaud to this conclusion, one asks what inducement he had to undertake them, perhaps, as an American, and an American representing his country in Europe, he does not like tiro discovery to be attributed to a Genoese navigator, but prefers that Providence should have made use of an unknown pilot. Doubtless his national pride makes him regret that some prehistoric American, some descendant of the Incas, did. not discover Europe and make it.an American conquest, instead of having to submit to tire-conquest- of America by European science and enterprise. However this may be, his book is full of new views and shows that there is much perspicacity
■and culture behind the-author’s ardent patriotism. General Marguerites, who distinguished himself in
Mexico and Africa, as well as in the Franco-1 German war, has left two sons, MM. Paul and Victor Margueritte, who are among the ablest and most esteemed of French writers. Sons of a hero, ardent patriots imbued with the virtues and warlike enthusiasm which were conspicuous in their father, they depict with eloquence and truth tho episodes of that war of 1870 which was the cradle of their name and which left them an inheritance of glory which they perpetuate by their writings. Their book, " Les Braves Gens,” published on Monday by Pion, containsaseries of narratives founded on the events of 1870-71 on the banks of the Moselle and the Loire. “ Les braves gens! ” was the well-known exclamation of the German victor when from tho heightsabovo Sedan he saw French soldiers rush on certain death in order to snatch at victory, despite the incapacity of their chiefs. The chapters are headed “ La ChovanchSe au Gouffre,” " Strasbourg,” “ Au SiSge de Paris,” " Sur la Loire.” All are full of interest. Some, like " Le Pigeon de Coulmieis,” are of touching melancholy. Others, such as " L’Hommo de Prills,” are full of humour and keen observation. Otters, again, like “ La Chevauchie au Gouffre,*’ give a striking and terrible picture of actual or imminent defeat. The entire volume is one of the finest works devoted to that campaign in which the French army, so thirsty for glory, was so poorly organized and commanded that individual effort was powerless.
—Our (him Correspondent.

NW CHAMBER MUSIC.
In sho usual absence of anything of very special interest in the way of concerted music published in England, such a work as the trio in B fiat minor, by Harvey Lohr (Schott and Co.), is likely to get quite as much attention from amateurs as it deserves. The work is exceedingly effective for all three instruments, and though the themes are not particularly distinguished, they are certainly very melodious, end are of a kind that will easily be recognized and enjoyed by casual hearers.—A book of “ Styrian Dances ” for piano and string quartet is arranged by C. Egerton Lowe from compositions of Josef Lanner, and is published by Novello
and Co. The sonata in E minor for violin and piano by A. Randegger, jun., is very interesting, written with fine command over the resources of both instruments and far above the average in regard to musicianship. The same composer's " Saltellato-caprice,'* dedicated te Kubelik, wants a Kubelik to do it justice; but hit " Souvenir ” will please a larger number of players. Arthur Hinton’s “Suite ” shows solid workmanship, knowledge of effect, and no little originality; Gustav von Holst’s " Greeting ” is very pretty and well written; and a set of pieces in canon throughout, by Battison Haynes, shows how cleverly learning can be disguised in a most acceptable form. All the “ Six Highland Dances” of J. B. McEwen have character, though some of the more subtle peculiarities ok Scottish music have hardly been brought forward. An arrangement of S. Coleridge-Taylor’s four “ Novelletten,” originally for stringed orchestra, is very well done, and the pieces should be quite effective in their new form. A “ Romance ” and a " Canzonetta," both in A major, both by Theodore S. Holland, and both dedicated to Professor Halir, are exceedingly graceful and not difficult. Four books of “ Btmto Blatter,” by Hans Sitt, containing in all 12 easy pieces fol violin and piano, are an addition of some importance to the number of compositions which do not require the violinist to go beyond the first position, and which are, at the same time, not uninteresting as music. F. H. Cowen’s “ Reverie ” is a graceful and expressive piece
of work. The series called “ The Junior Violinist “ contains some good selections from the classics, six sonatinas of Weber's, and some movements of string quartets by Mozart arranged for violin and piano; and “ The Modern School for the Violin ” has pieces of various degrees of difficulty, edited by A. Wflhelmj and James Brown. An instruction book for the viola, by Berthold Tours, is one of the firm’s useful primers.
A finely invented and really original sonata in G for violin and piano by W. Wolstenholme is the most interesting thing sent by Alfred Lengnick, who also issues a brilliant “ Rondo alia Zingarese,” for two violins and piano, by J. C. Ames.—It need not concern us to discuss the minute differences between Sir Frederick Bridge’s edition of Purcell’s violin sonata in G minor {Musical Sews office) aud that published some years ago under ths editorship of Mr. Alfred Moffat; it is a good thing that the sonata should be pnbliteed in any shape, and the variations of the text are so small that the two editions could quite well be used side by side.—A couple ok pretty fantasias on old Christmas carols, by Hans Sitt, are sent by Joseph Williams; it is only doubtfully correct to call the familiar “ O sanctissima ” a carol at all, but the name does not mute matter. The specimens sent of the same composer’s long series of 20 pieces, op. 73, are excellent of their kind, not at all excessively difficult, yet fresh in invention, and pleasing in idea. A graceful “ Arlette," and a pretty " Fouillo d’Album,” by A. d’Ambrosio, have just the qualities that amateurs like. Gabriel Pierne’s lively little “ Serenade a Colornbine ” has been arranged for violin and piano by William Henley, and is quite effective.—Landon Ronald's " Page d’Amour ” is another piece written with special view to Kubelik's playing,and is good practice as well as pleasing music.—Ths Vocalist is
a periodical publication in which a good many agreeable instrumsBtal pieces have appeared. Two melodies for violin and piano by J. M. Capal are among its latest issues, and both are quite acceptable formoderate players.
As usual, Messrs. Angener’s publications in the class of violin and violoncello music are exceedingly numerous ; for the first-named instrument there are three very graceful and effective pieces, op. 65, by Edmondstoune Duncan, a characteristic " HebridenTanz ” by A. Moffat, a brilliant concert-mazuria by W. Whitaker, a very taking “Orientals ” and a “ Romance " by A. d’Atabrosio, a tuneful “ Minnelied ” by J. L. Roeckel, a " Berceuse plaintive ” and “ AH' Ongarese " by G. Saint-George (both quite effective), and three attractive “ Morceaux de Salon ” by S. Esipoff. Haydn. Wood’s “ Souvenanco ” is a thoughtful and romantic little piece, and Horace Barton’s “ Romance ” is effective enough to servo its purpose ; Coleridge-Taylor’3 " Gipsy Suite,'’ op. 20, has all the originality and fine design that we are accustomed to look for in tins composer ; and if the little pieces by G. de Angelis are not of very great importance, M. Hauser’s transcriptions of favourite songs of Schubert will appeal to a large class of young players. An " Album Antique " of classical pieces, edited by A. Moffat, contains some unfamiliar things by masters of the ISth century that were well worth reviving ; various new editions of wellknown pieces by Ernst, David, Paganini, end ottœzs, appear; and forvioloncello and piano there are a vigorous sonata, op. 46, by X. Scharwerika, Mackenzie's pretty " Earghetto and allegretto,” op. 10, a pleasant little “ Ballade ” by Charles Quid, and a couple of wellwritten pieces by Hamilton Harty, as well as some transcriptions of well-known songs, such as Brahms’s “ Wie hist du, meins Kimigin,” by Sebastian
Lee and others. It is a little odd that Bech’s " My heart ever faithful ” should have been chosen, considering that Orcar Bruckner bus seen fit to make the violoncello play, not the obbligato part, but the melody, sadly to the detriment of the effect intended by Bach. Various books of studies, L<x, are also sent, besides a handy little mmiTtal of violin-playing translated from Carl Schroeder’s book, by J. Matthews.

LORD ROSEBERY’S NEW BOOK.
, , . ' '
Lord Rosebery has now finished a book dealing with the career of the great Lord Chatham, on which he has been engaged for a considerable time past. The book, which is entitled “ Chatham, His Early Life and Connexions,” is based to a very largo extent on material hitherto unpublished ; and it will bo issued on November 22 by Mr. Arthur L. Humphreys, of Piccadilly. It covers the early career ok Chatham, from his schooldays at Eton up "to the time when ho became an eminent Minister. Mr. Bovill Fortescue placed in Lord Rosebery’s hands the Dropmore papers, which were calendared some years ago for the Historical Manuscripts Commission, and. from this source much new material has been obtained. The book will contain character sketches of the principal figures of the period, such as George IL, Carteret, Walpole, Pultcnoy, and Bubb Dodington. It will be remembered that Lord Rosebery wrote a life of the younger Pitt in the English Statesmen Series.

. At this late hour we must content ourselves with only a sketch of the Fable,. which-has more intricacy Of plot than is usually given in an Opera.
The scjfe lies in Florence, and.the main incident arises from Octavio, a young1-nobleman os' that city, having redeemed a number of staves from Algiers, who open the piece' with a procession-and chorus.-
With these captives arc Regnalto and Montano ; the former a merchant of Florence, had’lelt his sister Viola with Aldobrand,, an old avaricious sawyer, who had contrived to keep his unsuspecting friend Regnalto in slavery, to become at dice possessed of Viola and her fortune.
Montano who had been seized by pirates and carried to Algiers, at the moment of his intended marriage with Laura, finds on returning, his beloved ns stress married to a Signor Lucilio, who is in fact her maid Rosa, whom Laura has per-. funded to assume that character, in order to put an end to the importunities of her numerous admirers.
Fi relock an old English soldier, whose favourite - maxim is, to lime all the days of his life, is made -use of tn several of the -situations,"though the plot does not entirely depend on him. This pleasant character was written, we understand, after the first reading of the Opera, and the author deserves considerable credit for the" ingenuity he has displayed in its introduction.
Regnalto is introduced in his slave’s habit into the house of Aldobrand, as the servant of Octavio, who, ignorant of his name and situation, gives a letter to Viola, settling the hour, of elopement. He pretends to be deaf and dumb, and thus becomes the witnesses Aldobrand’s artful endeavours to keep him still in slavery. -
Aldobr . -d though doting on his ward Viola, has still an inclination to. intrigue with Laura. He is persuaded by Alice to di (guise himself as a Confclibr, in order to gain an interview, which Mœy’ tane overhearing, forces him to change drclKs, with an intention by that means of upbraiding "his perfidious Laura with the infamy of her conduct. This gives birth to a number of comic incidents. -A variety of vexatious cross purposes happen to Aldobrand, till by a lucky hit he regains his ward Viola. But his joy is soon abated, by. the news of Regnalto being arrived from slavery, and which -he much dreaded.
Viola is at last seen descending from a balcony in Aldobrand’s garden, by moon-light, tp elope with Octavio. She is prevented by Aldobrand, who furprizes her. Regnalto, who now appears,, discovers himself. Montano is made -happy, by the supposed husband of Laura proving to lie a woman.-—Aldobrand’s villainy is exposed, and according to drarnatic custom, the lovers are limited.
Having thus slightly sketched out the Fable,' ■set us exainine the constituent-parts. The two "principal divisions are the drama and the music, which may be proporly considered the body and foul of Opeya." . ■ 1
The opening is finely imagined, but we thinks ‘it mivht be heightened by additional characters in .the procession, which would give contrast to the train of slaves. The scene .represents a distant 'view of the city of Florence, with the river Arno, .faid is extremely well executed.
As to the conduct of the Fable, we think it ex-' cellent in conception, though it sometimes fails in; action, owing-to-a redundancy- of-intrigue, not airways novel, and carried on like the pantomime comedies of the last century, by the assistance of disguises, concealments, listenings, and fide speeches. For this reason, we think the author: Was'prudentin converting his piece into an Opera, which does not require that strict adherence to na-. turn, and easy rise of. incident, indispensably ne'cessary to a comedy. " ; The author has materially transgressed the ‘rules of the drama in his catastrophe. . Fabio the villain—Fabio shouldn-Qt go unpunished—at least, ■ he should sty at the fight "of his injured friend.
We cannot conclude without’observing, that the "first representation of a drama, should be confidered as. the last rehearsal. The piece before Us is considerably too long, and particularly in the sc-- cond-act.' Let the scene Where the old man appears disguised be cut as short as possible. - * Notwithstanding those objections, it was received with general a»plause, apd if it sometimes strews * deficiency of judgment, it every where displays a strong genius. । Yesterday evening a new Farce, written by Mn O’Kceffej called A Beggar on Horseback, Wat presentedi for the first time, at the Theatre Royal
! in the Haymarket.
The fable of this piece is fin?p!e> and well conceived : an old gentleman falls in love with Nannjq a country girl, who, with her broth* Corney> he takes- into his house as sesvantg, but influenced by his paflion, he treats the object of it with every mark of tenderness. Master Gorney, an ignorant* yet cunning and vicious clown* uses the power ot" his stltex’s'beauty over her antiquated gallant, as a means to promote his evrix interest, and in the various capacities of footraatf> coachman, and butter, gets into the Most ludicrous and laughable situations. The old gentleman has a nephew named Horace* who having acted in the country, for a frolic, had there gained the heart of Nanny, whom he meets ar hie Uncle's house. This produces a train of farcical incidents, and the piece terminates with a marriage between this young gentleman dud hit uncle's maid.
The first act of this pent piece, considering it tn the Jine of farce, is unexceptionable in dialogue, incident, and business. In the second the author has not been so successful; for though the design is good, the execution fails, which may be imputed rather to hurry than want of art; and a pasiage or two in the dialogue was found offensive to some over delicate ears : these, however* may be expunged.
Gorney, who is the Beggar on Horseback, was played by Edwin ; and never did beggar ride hit steed with greater success. He was a beggar of rich humour, o\xt-beggarixg description. Upon the whole, it is our opinion, this farce merits, and will experience a very considerable run, istit undergoes a few alterations.
The other performers, particularly Mrs. Wells* did ample justice to their author.

Of all forts of outlaws, that set of men called smugglers are the most pernicious,by infecting the manners of men ; for, while" they practise most of those vices that generally go hand in hand with unlawful and underhand dealing, they are less anxious, to conceal their actions; their occupation not happening to be branded with the fame degree of infamy-that house-breaking, or.other theft is ; they mix with their fellow* subjects, While at the fame time, they are guilty of those things that are generally attended with every species of vice.
Men are always, and always will be, influenced by .custom and example in their opinions; but as custom and example must .owe their existence to natural propensities, or some other cause, it would seem that men do not consider positive theft, which consists: in the taking what is really-in the possession of another man, to be of the same nature with. that kind of theft that conceals things you have otherwise got posteffion of, and ought to deliver up. - Smuggling certainly is of this latter sort of theft, attended often with the risque of murder, and those other desperate actions "that increase rhe crime.

Reducing the height of houses in Paris to the proposition of breadth of street, will not be insisted upon by the Crown of France without indemnity. A valuation will be made of this upper property, and paid by Goverment. A certain portion of the city will be thus reformed every year. This will encourage wider streets, that will admit of footways—a circumstance the French are enthusiastic upon.
Before, however, this can take place in many parts of Europe, the roads must be put in'a state of preservation. This can only be done by a general broad-wheel,- proportioned to the several classes.of carriages, including those of artillery. This method will preserve and make serviceable all the roads throughout the world.

A new Pantomime, called, Hers and There atid Every Where was exhibited sot the first- time last flight, at the- Theatre Royal, Haymarket. " . ,
It opens with a. pleisantprelude, in which the. siupposed author of the pi$ce observes, that comedy has stolen her -tricks from pantomime— that the Genius of 1stonstxfc is at one side of the Hay-market, and the Tfonfenfe of Genius at the other, alluding to pantomime on an English stage, and Shakespeare’^reduced.to an Italian dance; He then hurries to the theatre, observing to a friend, that having pawned his vjafch, .he has Hyfcd upon ‘tick ever" since; * ' .
We cannot fay that this piece'abounds in either humour, drollery, or novelty; there is one beautiful scene of a fog after the manner of Loutherbdurgh in his Eudiphosikon,, and Meadows* imitation of the birds .13 astonishing.— The change of.Buzaglio’s hospital to the dancing dogs at Sadler’s Wells Is happily executed, .but we cannotfry that the boys are so clever in their imitations of the canine, as the dags are in the humanfpecies,md it is very remarkable, that though the- prelude opens- frith an account of the author’s 1 practising leaps, there is not a leap in the whole piece.

Sample 129:
==================================================

THA
.
The stage is an object of the - highest importance in every free state, and-the experience of his*iory proves, that liberty has always made her last stand in the Theatre. This accounts for the protection which theatrical entertainments have everreceived from the people-of this country# and this will sufficiently justify us, in thus declaring that the Universal Register shall be found a firm, and warm advocate for . every author, who.keeps the great end of the drama in view, that is, who holds the mirror up to-nature. -
On the other hand we shall never gite our assistance to inexplicable. dullness and dumb strew; vain splendor without real utility, and low buffoonery destitute ot morality, shall ever be treated with that censure and reprobation they, deserve.
The stage is a medium of instruction, which should never be corrupted with impunity, and the people have an interest in its purity ..that never can be aliened.- It*is an instructor that rectifies morals, by representing the rewards of virtue and punishments of vice.. Its examples infuse courage into the soldier, increases the zeal of the patriot, and instills a love and admiration of virtue into every class. Men lowly educated, are seldom acquainted even with the general maxims of morality, but in a well regulated theatre, they imbibe them by the strength of example. Such mtnds require visible illustrations : and these benefits can only be received from theatrical representations#
The elevated lights of philosophy are too strong for-the weak eye of the-vulgar. Tell an ignorant man, that happiness conlists not in the possession of wealth—preach to him that virtue ought Zo seek recompense in itself, and that no interest should persuade a man to do a base, or a mean thing, and he will consider you as stating paradoxes. This makes such men suspect philosophy and even religion. They must therefore be instructed by a means which immediately affects the senses, and such means can only be found on the stage, which has, therefore, been properly filled the School est the People,
In dramatic pieces, exhibited in this school, one indispensable rule Ihould exist, and every deviation from it should be discountenanced. Virtue should be always commended and rewarded, in spightofthc injuries of fortune, and vice never escape without chastisement, or at least without detestation, though crownedWith success.
Asta$e regulated upon this principle can teach every thing morefenfibly, by the power of representation, than philosophy can pretend to, by the force of precept, for on the stage the meanest capacity may visibly fey that the favours of fortune arc not real enjoyments and that the sufferings of the virtuous arc but temporary. They have before theit* eyes the example of calamities—they are Convinced that heaven inflicts remorse and mental tortures upon those whom the laws cannot reach with*corporal puniibmeht.
It is on the stage the people view the ambitious man tormented by his own conscience. The impious wretch, driven to despair by the furies within his own breast-*^they fee the tyrant# who haying engaged in crimes to attain his ends, and after violating the laws of Heaven and earth, fall into misfortunes as great as those he had inflicted upon others, and rendered more unhappy by himself than by his enemies#
It is on the stage that covetousness appears a disease of the foul, the miser is shewn persecuted with continual restlessness, starving in the midst of plenty, from an apprehention that he might. hereafter want. In short, it is the art of the drama to penetrate into the hidden secrets of human najta're, and to touch the foul with a picture of living truths. Few indeed depart from a theatre without carrying with them an idea of the persons represented ; a knowledge of those virtues and vices, of which they have seen the examples and having their minds improved by the moral of the piece.
But, were the exhibitions of the theatre merely nmhfcments, • incapable of conveying instruction, their utility would be considerable by preventing the commission of crimes' -;nd debaucheries. In all great cities there are persons who have jio occasion to apply to lab )ur for support; to such persons, public entertainments are the most innocent mpdesof employment, and it must be allowed, there are no places wherein the trader or-artizan can spend their hours of relaxation to so much advantage. They give business to the idle, and pleasure to the industrious.
These reasons will justify us in declaring ourselves friends to the drama, and giving it every aid ih our power.
In the progress of this task, it will often fall to out lot to pronounce opinions on dramatic pieces and performers. In the execution of our judgments, we .shall strew a liberal disquisition of the merits, and with that impartial justice which is the leading principle of criticism, point out their beauties as well as blemishes.
OPENING of the THEATRES.
The theatrical season commenced on Saturday evening at Drury-Lane. The play Othello, the entertainment the Quaker, Notwithstanding the warmth of the weather, the house was croudcd in every part at first price,-and at after price tlyerc was a very considerable overflow, a circumstance which proves-thnt the attracting influence of Mrs. Siddons is strong as ever. The audience was not only numerous, but brilliant, many persons of the" first distinction being present.
The theatre has not undergone any material alteration, nor has it been embellished with any hew ornaments; nor does it, in our opinion, require any.
Of the play and of the performers it would be superfluous to fay much. 'Othello is Shakespeare’s, and Mrs. Siddons was his Desdemona in most situations, though not every where softened into the gentle.
Kemble appeared much improved, and though his voice often* failed," yet in his acting there were strong marks both of genius and judgment.
Half price being again come round, the city bucks drive westward hoa! tn the evening, and the box lobby of the theatres, appear a la militaire with cQckadcd troops of half pay officers*

THEATRE.
In our observations oh-the stage, we hare long over-looked several objects of material confideration|in the regulation of 1 theatre.— The plays and the actors suffer every- day under ill-natured animadversions, while the audience, who are for ever offending, generally escape with impunity—their conduct is the subject of this essay, and I shalt divide them into classes.
THE BOXES.
. With the bright circle that decorates the fide and front boxes, little fault can be found; for though a groups of females sometimes converse ur tones above the actors, they are generally homely tabbies, or antiquated fusty maidens, whose personal charms failing to attract admiration, determine them to draw the eyes of the audience upon them by noise : But let them examine the looks "of those who notice theircoaduct, and they will nor have much to boast of, they will fee contempt and indignation depicted on every countenance.
As.to the gentlemen who frequent this part of the-theatre, if not all men of fense, they are most ok them men of breeding, if we except the half price bucks, but they, however seldom appear except in the lobby or green boxes!
GREEN BOXES.
These boxes may be likened to those parts in some of the foreign theatres on the Continent,' where women of certain free description arc ad* mined ; but with this material difference, that in this country women of good character frequently, mix with these dif_i plcs of Venus the tnereirici-" mis, a circumstance which should have seme weiebt even with the molt proffig:tc of oui blood:j and relirain them from offending the chaste ear of the innocent virgin and honest matron with inifeLcnc language.
THE PIT.
The pit audience is composed of periods' where' the poet describes.;
•• More firmed against than finning." because they are lionets harmless people, generally pretending to a great deal of wit, bur peflctting a considerable share of simplicity, a,id toerefore their principal object is, to receive In entertainment, value for what they pay at the door. The pit, however, is not without its faults, its tenants often joining the galleries in encoring long, and laborious songs, and opposing reasonable demands by no, no, and off, off, to the great discomfiture of the performer.
FIRST GALLERY. .
This gallery is frequently alarmed by fracas from drunken ruffians and abandoned prostitutes# but these disturbances, it must be allowed, are less frequent than heretofore.
second gallery.
This is a region which, though last mentioned; is not least in notice. Its frequenters often insult the pit, and with impunity, availing themselves like others in high life of their exalted stations, to throw and scatter indignities upon those beneath them, which they do in the shape ok orange peels and apples- These gentry are never contented with the-bill of fare held out by the manager, but v-aCiferate for Roajl Beef, Peas upon a Trencher, or the Brown J ugg, and often indeed become actors themselves by entering into loud conversations of low humour, and joining in fungs anst chorus.

K ING's THEATRE.
This Theatre was crovded on Saturday evening in every parr; the overflow from the pit was so extraordinary that numbers of those who were di (appointed of places got upon the stage, which impeded the performance, and gave offence to those in front. On the rising of the curtain a cry of “ off, off!” commenced from all parts of the theatre ; but those who had possession of the stage persevered in keeping it, and Gallini whp was called for, and came forward, was pulled back by them. Mara made an attempt to speak, but could not be heard ; violence and clamour overpowering every attempt made to establish peace. It being impossible to perform (he Opera with any effect, the dancers came on, but the uproar still continued, and several apples and oranges were thrown, till at last the curtain fell, and the audience broke up in tumult and confusion.

THEATRE,
COVENT-GARDEN.
All In the Wrong, with the Cheats of Scapin,
This theatre opened with particular eclat after the recent suppression, to a very croud.’d, but the universal fables forbid us fay, a brilliant audience, The novelty of the night, Mrs. Belfille, from the Norwich theatre, in the part of Belinda. This lady’s figure is striking and elegant—her face is beautiful, and her.manner, if we consider the embarrassment of a first appearance, sufficiently easy. In .addition to these requisites, her e - nunciation is clear and distinct: many of her sprightly passages had merit, and her pathos was not without its force. Her manner in some scenes resembled that of Mrs. Abington, undebased, however, by the servility of a copyist. Weihuse to speak thus negatively of this lady’s merits, and lu'pend our judgment, because we think, with her powers file may do more. The iii'fr appearance, particularly of an elegant female, is too unc. rtain a test to forma decided opinion of the degree of excellence flic may be found to
possess-
Mis. Mattocks in Lady Restless, had all the traits of discriminated humour and vivacity which usually distinguish her performance, and Lewis in Beverly gave many scenes with uncommon effect, particularly that in the fourth act, where his fondness breaking out through his resentment, was given with the happiest force of transition.
■ Mr. Ryder in Sir John Restless, deserved a very high degree of commendation. The comic scenes in general were enriched with great humour, particularly the Park scene, where the compart' son Beverly with his picture, the absurd questions to detain him, the “ what length is the voyage to Botany Bay”'—all arose from the occ<lion, and shewed the judgment, while they evinced the powers of the performer.
His Scapin, as it shewed him in a new light, demands a different kind of praise. The spirit os arch intrigue and low humour, pervaded and recommended the whole of the performance ; and frotn the bursts of applause which accompanies his personifying the different characters, while beating Gripe in the sack, we -are apt to imagine that this force, however extravagantly laughable, is likely to become a favourite with the audience.

'THEATRE.
Last night their Majesties, with their usual Tuif, accompanied by the Princess Royal and Princess Augusta, were at the Theatre Royal, Drury-lane, to fee the Tragedy of Tancred and Mgismunda, and the Critic, which were performed to a brilliant and croud ed audience. Every , fresh appearance of Mrs. Sidd->ns adds laurels to her f ime ; nor was (lie less happy in Sigifinunda, than in any other character she has hitherto performed—whether we consider her voice, attitude, "or looks, every step she takes, every word she speaks, and every cast of countenance is chaste and interesting to the auditor—She has a cry of distress which steals imperceptibly into the heart, and rivets the affection. We think the language Df this Tragedy will admit of frequent representation, and interest the public in its success—We have alwavs considered it as interesting in the closet, and we think Mrs. Siddons will render it equally so on the stage. Mrs. Brereton, in justice to her merit should not be palled in silence, as Hie was respectable in her character; and Mr. Kemble, though not so generally nervous, was great in several of the scenes, particularly the last; mid was his voice equally powerful with his conception of the parts he undertakes, we might pronounce him not inferior .to many who have appeared before him—Mr. Barrymore, in the second parts of Tragedy, may be considered as an useful "actor,—his Osman was well performed, though his countenance is not strongly marked, his voice and action are not displeasing—the former powerful.

T H E A T R - E.
:COVEN'T-GAKDEN1
Last * night a new opera called Richard Cceur A Lion was presented tor the first time at this theatre. The circumstance of its being taken from the English History, yet.performed originally at a French Theatre, the rivaUhip on the subject announcL-d between our two theatres, all. raised a degree of expectation which it was- extremely difficult to gtatify; yet this effect was produced in a very great degree,, and the most crouded audience we have witnessed this season were extremely liberal of dr strongest marks of approbation.
DRAMATIS- PERSOH.Æ.
Richard 1. Cœur. de Lion, 1 »- T uc '-King of England, J Mr. Inchbald. ■
Blondel, his confidential friend, Mr. Johnstone. Florestine, Governor of a j Mr. Dlvies.

■ Knights, Soldiers, Peasants, &c.
Scene, a Castle and its vicinity, situated in a forest in Germany.
The subject of this opera was originally dramatized in France, and brought out at the Comedic Liaiienne as a musical comedy, and its success was very considerable, owing to the music, particularly the chorustes, which are remarkably fine.
The piece before us is a regular opera, and to adopt the words of Puff, is not translated, but fatten from the French. Several of the characters, the dialogue, and most of the songs, being new.
The English author has also avoided several gross anacronisms, and monstrous improbabilities, ■ which appear in the French.
TJie dialogue breathes a spirit of liberty and loyalty.
' 'The French author has not attempted one character ot humour. The English author has introduced La Bruce, Berghen, Margery, and Lauretta, which character in the original is sentimental.
The French music is retained—the music introd need is from Anfoffi, Corolan, and David Rizzio. The new music by Shield.
The fable is simply this : Richard the First returning from the Holy Land, is made prisoner by Leopold Duke of Austria, who basely delivers him up to his enemy, Henry Emperor of Germany. Henry confines him in a strong castle, to the care of a brave and honest soldier named Florestine ; but the place of his confinement is kept secret. Morgan, a elstl soldier, having steel from England with Margery and Lauretta, hiswife and daughter, on account of transgressing the forest laws, commences publican in the neigh bourhood of the castle. Berengeria, Queen to Richard, and Blondel his friend, came to this bouse unknown to each other ; the Queen pretend-.. she is going to retire into a nunnery— Blondel under the disguise of an old blind minstrel. - Florestine the Governor being in love with Lauretta, sends her a letter by Guillot, which her father intercepts ; and it being read in the hearing of Blondel, it comes out that there is a stranger closely confined in the castle; which induces Blondel to suspect that Richard is the prisoner. In consequence of this suspicion, he mounts the parapet of the castle, and sings an ode of Richard’s composing ; to which Richard answers from a terrace ; but at the instant of the discovery, the soldiers rush from a postern of the castle, and seize Blondel. The Governor coming out at the same time, Blondel assures him, that his bustness was to deliver a message from Lauretta to him ; and the Governor promises to come at night to her father’s, where a wedding is to be celebrated. The Queen having disc >vered Blondel, they admit Morgan into the secret; and on the Governor’s arrival he is seized by the asst stance of her knights, and the keys of the castle , taken from him ; by which Blondel, Moigan, and the Knights get admission to the castle, overpower the guards, and relet le their Sovereign.
From this sketch of the bustnest, our readers will fee, that there is a continued and progressive interest which increases gradually to the conclusion. The unities of place and time are well preserved: and what we have rarely seen in productions of this nature. The under-plot is strictly connected with the upper, by makT.g Lauretta daughter to Morgan, the instrument of the King’s ■ deliverance. There is also a vein of pleasantry running through the whole, not forced or artificial, but evidently growing from the bustness of the scene.
The piece was supported, by all the operatical strength of the Theatre. An apology was made for Mrs. Billington, who we were informed, was seized with a sudden hoarse nest. For this, how.-ever, we scarcely saw occaston ; there appeared, indeed, some little deprestion of her powers in . her first song; but in her -first long in the third .’act, (hefar exceeds our praise. The richness of . her melody, the extent and variety of her cadences were so many claims on our most rapturous approbation, and stamped her the first finger that ever graced an English stage. Her song .also, beginning, “ Freedom, divine, -ethereal . -Same,” was executed in a style of excellence peculiar to herself, though not so well calculated as the former, to shew the power of her voice.
' Mrs. Martyr was extremely well, particularly in her song with staccato accompaniments, in the second act, and in the duet with Johnstone, which
. immediately succeeded.
Johnstone exerted himself with an energy . which.did him honour. His-fongs, “ Oh, sweet remembrance! airy sprite,” and that beginning, “ Surrounded in the thickest fight,’ whereby he discovers his Sovereign, were both in his best manner.- Edwin in La Bruce was seen to advantage, and he repayed the author for the situation he had afforded him, by the strongest exertions of
-
h*s wonted Ability. Had 'Mrs.- KctweJy an J Mrs., Brown been oftner introduced, it would have added to the pleasure of the audience.
The piece appears to be got up with a particular degree of attention-—The d-inces and scenery were well silked to. the occasion-r-on .the whole we are of opinion; that with a sparing use of the pruning knife^ RicbatdCtsnr de 'Lion will become a favourite with .the town, rmd long maintain its fitutrion. It was'.giveniout with a very, trifling opposition for Wednesday.

(sty d Correspondents)
RICHARD COEUR DE LION.
b
lL
e pubc expectation-was last? night amply gratified, by the appearance of Richard Cater de Lion, at this Theatre. Th6 double anticipation of this legendary tale, on the French Stage, and finch on that of Co vent-Garden, has rendered it a subject of such publicity,, that it becomes unnecessary for us to repeat the particulars.
The principal deviation from the original, as it was last night exhibited, is that of effecting the deliverance of Richard, by the immediate exertions of his Queen Matilda, under the disguise of a minstrel, instead of Blondel, to whom the French
author consigned it. Thus by making her love and conjugal fidelity, which rises superior to every difficulty, the substitute for the friendship of a faithful follower, not only the interest and pathos are extremely heightened, but a field opened for the display of Mrs. Jyiidan’s judgment and pleasmg precirion in speaking, and tor the best exertion of her vocal powers. A few other minute, but critical deviations, prevent all dullness of narration, and effect a rapid and lively representation, of the translation we can with great j office say, that the dialogue and poetry evidently discover their being the production o! first rate abilities; and the adaptation of the music to the words, a d the talents of rhe several performers, as strongiy characterize the judgment of a Linliy, as the composition itself does rhe taste and elegance of a Gretiy. The merits of this excellent master are sufficiently known to the public, by the perfonnance of tae piece -at tne other house, and it could
loss nothing oy. being adapted to the poetical harmony of th! Songs in the present performance, Tim scenery certainly does the highest credit to the invention and abilities of that very skilful artist Mr, Greenwood it is not confined merely to rhe effect of the pencil, producing a pci fpective upon a flat ; but following the example of Loutherhourg, he covers the whole stage with l.enery, tnd gives the utmost effi-ct the arr is cap,hie of producing. It has for several days been rumoured upon ’Change, that a hint which we some time fiiice offered will be adopted ; namely, for the mercantile and manufacturing interest to appoint a Remembrancer, whose duty at all times is to watch the motions of Parliament, and of Ministers, in public treaties &c. and to give the earliest notice to all the parties concerned throughout the kingdom. If such an appointment really do s take place, it cannot but be attended with the most beneficial consequences.

bfg wh^ar, bailey, oats, and all other feeds, with tka tSl. Ill , ST r» VA’...TnrslB 1-1 zv « r . z4 Zll- ..-lli -.2-
the help of a vegetable powder, which will not only s.,ve one half of rhe u u a quantity of seed in rhe first instance, but will likewise be the means of
producing a greater quant ty in tire crop. The corn, will be huger, heavier, ana tield more flour than when sown in the common way, with double the quantity of feed. Experiments have been tried in the environs of Paris, in rhe pretence of the Duke of Orleans, rb- Duke FitzJames, the Comte de Provence, and Mont. Dtrcrot de Bclbader, in the fame fields, and the following was the result:—Duke Fitz James from fix bushels of feed sown reaped ten more bushels •than from the twelve bushels of seed sown in the common method; the Duke, the Comte, and Mons'. Ducrot de Belbader, were all equally successful, and reaped nearly in the same proportion. It is also asserted, that this powder will produce tolerable crops even upon barren land, and has the fame virtue of increasing grass, restoring consumptive plants, and all sorts of flowers, vegetables, &c. &c. Of what ingredients this powder is composed, or whether it preserves those qualities here boasted of, must be left to time to determine.

A letter from Bristol, dated Oct. 20, fays, “ There is a talk here that Government are about to purchase the Isle of Lundy, which is below King Road in this channel ; for what purpose is not known, whether for building a Lazaretto, or to confine mid employ felons.”
This day the sessions at the Old-Baiky will commence, at which upwards of 160 felons arc to take their trials, ft-veral of whom are charged with verv heinous offences.
On Friday in rhe afternoon as Mr. Williamson was-coming rrom Oxford in a post chaise, he was stopped on Houmiow-heath, by two high wavmen ; one stood before the driver of the chaise with a pistol in his band, while the other robbed Mr. Williamson of eight guineas, then bid the .driver go on ; they were both young men, and well mounted ; one on a sorrel, the other on a bay horse.

Monday morn'ng her Grace the Duchess of Portl md was safely delivered of a still born childat Burlington House. We are happy to hear her Grace is as well as can be expected.
On Sunday was married, by the Lord Bishop of Chester, by special licence, Lieutenant General Fawcett, Adjutant General, to Mrs. Stinton, of Winton.
On Wednesday died at Blackheath, in an advanced age, Mrs. Susannah Crawford, neice to the late Earl of Dartmouth.

Edinburgh, Oil- 18. At a meeting of the Directors of the Chamber of Commerce and Manufactures, held here this day, a conversation took place respecting an intended alteration in the corn laws of this kingdom. The Directors were unanimously of opinion, that great abuses have obtained during the operation of the present laws, in regard to opening and shutting die ports ; but the new arrangement being vet in embryo, they adjourned the further configuration of the business till Wednesday the first of November next.

THEATRE.
DRURY-LANE.
. On Saturday evening this theatre opened with, the School for Scandal and the farce of All the World's a Stage. The opening with the first of these evinced the greatest judgment in the managers. No play could be more proper for that purpose, as it comprises nearly the whole comic force of the company, and what is yet more, it is happily adapted to the forte of those whom it includes. Often as we have seen this admirable comedy, we confess that, on Saturday night, it grew upon our estimation. The performers are all so mellowed in their respective parts, and they exerted themselves with so much spirit, that every’ point was given with new force, and the delusion of the scene- was perfectly : complete. The audience received their old favourites .with unbounded marks of applause ; but cf these' the greatest share fell to Mess'. King, Palmer, Parsons. Smith, and Miss Farren. Mrs-Incbbaid played Lady Sneerwell, in the absence of Mrs. Ward, and her performance gave us no reason to regret the substitute.
The screen scene wav one of the finest pieces cf. acting which ever appeared on the stage, whether we notice the feelings or attitudes of King, Smith, Palmer, or Miss Farren ; and was a Hogarth now alive to pencil their characters, it would, be one of the most striking prints ever exhibitedon the boards of either theatre.
The House was extremely crouded ; and among, other persons of fashion we recognized, the Portugueze Ambassador, General Paoli, and a party of foreigners who filled the Prince’s box. Air, Sheridan, with his Lady, were also present, and seemed greatly to enjoy the hilarity which his genius had created and diffused.
ALL THE WORLD’S A STAGE.
We recommend to the Managers never to give so trifling an after-piece, after such an excellent play, but rather to diversify it with music or pantomime. Baddeley took occasion to make an allusion to the late tremendous incident of the desert, knife, which he compared to the silver spoon of the mock tragedy. Perhaps Baddeley looks to the felicity ot carrying up an address, and to clap his sickle into the present plenteous harvest of honours. If' so. Sir John Baddeley would certainly cut a figure in the bills.

THEATRE.
COVENT-GARDEN.
(By a Correspondents)
Last night this Theatre opened with the Belles Stratagem, and the Farce of Patrick in Pruilia. This Comedy has in general more show than" intrinsic merit, and the sentiments which occur are raised too far beyond the tone of conversation. These defects, however, were overlooked in the excellence of the performance, and it was played, to a crouded and brilliant audience, with incessant marks of approbation.
Of the attractions of the night, the fl rouges! was the return of Mrs. Pope to the stage, after the absence of a'fenson. Her health seems fully reestablished, and she gave to her Letitia even more than its accustomed charms. In the expressive language of Brooke, " her looks were elegance, her movements grace.”
The return of Mrs. Mattocks to a London stage, was also honoured with an earnest welcome, which file requited by playing the Widow Racket with spirit and propriety. Mr. Farren in Sir George Touchwood, and Mrs. Wells in Lady Frances were highly respectable.
Mr. M’Crcady, who made his first appearance in Hutton, though labouring under an evident' embarrassment, acquitted himself with much- propriety : as far as we could judge, when a little time has. worn off those petty habits, on which we " are obliged to comment in every provincial actor, be will become an useful acquisition to the Theatre.'-" -
-The humourous improbabilities of Patrick its Pruffia, aided by the melody of Johnstone, and the comicality of Edwin, difmifled the audience in perfect good humour with the entertainment and rvith themselves.

ALTHOUGH Mr. Palmer has not had the fessod fortune to succeed in his original scheme of performing' tegular-dramatic, pieces,'yet by his judicious management, and Wonderful- exertions, ■he is enabled to; and at the fame time does, afford infinitely greater novelty, than any theatre of the present day. -The ttoldiiess of this assertion is, without dispute, rather presumptuous, though the truth of it, I am persuaded, will be stilly established, by the brilliant company-that nightly honour his theatre with their presence. The performances of late have been varied every evening, which at once evinces his indefatigable assiduity to please every palate. His exhibitions at this time may certainly vie with the most powerful competitors; particularly in the vocal line, in which he doubtless may lay a just claim to pre-eminence. The whole of his entertainments are replete with wit, humour, and the keenest satire; so that they form a delightful repast to every lover of mirth. But, above all, the grand Tragic-Pantomimic performance of Don Juan, exceeds every thing of the kind ever produced ; the revival of which .redounds greatly to Dclpini's credit; for it must be confessed, it is got up in a most superb and elegant sslle. The scenery is beautiful beyond description. We think, if any one scene has the preference, it is the view of the storm and shipwreck, which, in truth, is finely executed. Bur we cannot dismiss this article without mention of Mr. Palmer’s inimitable representation of Don Juan ; as it is our firm opinion, that the character cannot have the dignity of person more strongly featured, than in this unrivalled performer. The different situations of "mirth, gaiety, and horror, are delineated to perfection. In short, from the specimens- Mr. Palmer has already given of his unremitting attention for the amusement of die public, the greatest expectations may be entertained of his future fucc.-fs.

THEATRE;
Yesterday evening a Lady, named Pollard, made 1 her first appearance on the stage of the TheatreRoyal, Drilry-Lnnc, in the tragedyiof the Orphan —the character Monimia.
We recollect to have seen this Lady perforin Erixine, in the Brothers, at a private performance, exhibited at the Lyceum, in the Strand, and .we apprehend the encouragement site received on that occasion from a private audience, seduced her to try her powers before the public.
As we cannot bestow any praise on this Lady’s performance of last night, we forbear the unwelcome-talk of a critical examination. Suffice it to fay, that her talents are of the very middling fort indeed.
In the name of wonder—O Kemble ! why attempt Castalio ?—Chamont was the part which judgment should have pointed out.

The Wynneftay Theatre goes on bufily. MrSheridan was very much pressed by his jolly host to take a part in the Tempe fl, but he excused himself on the score of those preparations he was under the necessity’of making for engaging in something of a similar drama, on another stage- It is laid that he longs for the part of Prospero—but it if thought that his wishes will not be gratified.
To the common and universal question of— What news?—it may be now said, with a degree of truth, and a propriety of application which the general answer has not always possessed,—rhof there is no news in the World.
How great names fink into little ones! Mr, Wilkes was so ill that he could not attend his Wardmote, on St. Thomas’s Day, and no ens seems to know anything about his recovery. After all the buttle and noise that he has made is this kingdom, if he did not possess a place which would occasion some fort of hurly burly in the city—he would probably go out of the world with as little, or even less noise, than he came into it.
May it. not be supposed, with some degree of reason, that the appointment of a successor to Lord Mansfield hangs on. the condition of Lord Stormont’s giving the strength of his abilities to Administration ? and would it not, in the prefeat situation of affairs, answer a good purpose to Government, if the noble Judge’s inclination was gratified, on such a condition being fulfilled ?
Are the enmities of people of fashion as shortlived as their friendships;—or can the trillings of an eunuch subdue the rancour of fashionable bosoms ?-Something of this fort must be the cafe, as two well known ladies of difilnSion were seen at the opera the ether evening, engaged in ail the warmth of friendly communication, who, during the last winter, were continually excommunicating each other by bell, book, and wax lights.
The Queen’s Concerts, at Buckingham-house^ are to commence on the Monday evening otter the I Sth of January, which is observed as her Majesty’s birth-day. There are to be only four. The last of them will be in May.
The land called ths Isle of Dogs should be ertirely insulated, and appropriated for ship-building til round it: the center, warehouses and dwellinghouses. It would be also beneficial in shortening the navigation to London for shipping. The Kent fide of the river might be devoted to wet and cry docks, if any more are wanted.
As the harbours of England have cast many millions of money, without cue being in the least perfect, it would be best to let them remain as they are, until Parliamentary Commissioners are appointed, who receiving information from til qua:ters, would gain a certain knowledge how to proceed, in creating new, or reforming die old. There does not 'ppeara want of money, but of judgment. The Thames shores, between Deptford and London, should have occasional semi o.-ali, that ships might float in twenty feet water, out of the tide-way ; for want of these they suffer forty thousand pounds a year every frost in cables. Besides this, shipping, in losing an hour every rise, -by the abuse of London-Bridge,do, by calculation, suffer one hundred thousand pounds. Titis, in a century, amounts to fourteen millions of money. Would the reform of the Thames, and the rebuilding of London-Bridge, under proper en* gineers, with the City and Trinity revenues added, exceed this astonishing sum i

Ro r a l z z theatre,
Jalyt, 2787.
Mr. PALMER, proposing to open his Theatre to-morrow evenpig, with rhe utmost deference presumes to solicit the patronage of that public, to whose indulgent kindness he is already to much indebted, and on the continuance of whose favour lie places his sole reliance. Had Mr. Palmer experienced the fame lenient forbearance, by which the theatre at Birmingham, q nd many others in the Itingdom, are enabled to exhibit dramatic pieces, his exertions, as an actor, would have been unremitted. That not being the cafe, Mr. Palmer submits most chcarful ly to the laws of his country, however he may be detrimented by the severity of their oper tion. Although, through the friendly cflices of Mr. Harris, Mr. Palmer has been marked, in his theatrical capacity, as a rogue, a •vagaioad, and “ sturdy beggar—yet, as a member of the community at large, it has been his wish to discharge the relative duties becoming a good citizen.
Under the authority by which the RoyaltyTheatre will now be opened, Mr. Palmer will offer that species of entertainment, which shall have novelty to attract, and, he humbly hopes, excellence to please, those who may honour his house with their presence. ■ Rendered incapable of executing the whole of his design, his utmost powers shall he used to render the partial system left him, .perfect in its kind. Those who know the difficulties which Mr. Palmer hath had to encounter, are sensible, that even to open his theatre on any plan 6f respectability, is matter of surprize, rather than of hope; for there is not a stogie performer engaged by Mr. Palmer, whom-Mr. Harris hath not.personally endeavoured to dissuade from appearing on his boards.
• Ina defection thus caused, Mr. Charles Bannisteronly hath shewn himself a man, neither to - be seduced by flattery, nor intimidated by frowns.
Attached to Mr. Palmer, as a member of the fame profession, he hath been firm' at a jisque, when others have wavered through interest. Such characters, in whatever line, mdst endear themselves to the public, as long as friendship has a patron in the world.
Mr. Palmer will not trespass longeron the publie. in his novel undertaking he'has experiencedevery discouragement, and a great deal of obloquy. The last, when proceeding from anonymous pens, he considers not of sufficient authority to merit notice. The discouragements thrown in his way, Mr. Palmer hath endeavoured, to the utmost of his power, to surmount. His object is, as a man, living by public estimation, to render himself generally acceptable in his profeffianal capacity ; find an indulgent public will, he doubts not, by their generous patronage, enable him to triumph over his enemies, and finally render his efforts successful.
JOHN PALMER.

Royalty '.Theatre, July 12.
Mr. PALMER, highly sensible of the diiHno guiflied favour conferred on him by the Nobility, who have lately honoured his theatre with their presence, begs permission thus to return his public thanks, and to express the very grateful sensation excited in his mind, by so condescending a thark of kindness. Circumscribed as Mr. Pal* mer at present is, with respect to the nature of his entertainments, -he will endeavour to make up, in variety and excellence, what may be wanting in theatrical species; and rhe patronage of the nobility, and generous support of the public, which .Mr. Palmer, now experiences, operates as additional inducements for him to exert every possible means to render the amusements of the Royalty Theatre superiorly attractive.
The Tale from " Baker’s Chronicle,” and the recital of " Collins’s Ode on the Passions/’ having been received with, such general applause, Mr. Palmer will continue them this and every evening, until public notice to the contrary bs given.
. Mr. Palmer once more humbly presumes Cd ent ».it the Nobility and Gentry, who mean to honour the Royalty Theatre with ilieir presence, to fend as early as poffible to the Box-deeper for places, that they may experience the best accommodations the house will afford.
Mr. Palmer will very soon have a large vacant space of ground, adjoining the Royalty Theatre, prepared as a recess for carriages ; a convenience which, he flutters himself, will prove highly ad* vantagcous*

Alex- Stevens ; in some passages he excels all -his' cotemporaries,-’since the death of that favourite son of. mirth 4 in others, however, he is rather too impassioned1; but this seems more to arise fromisl habit, than a natural defect; and we have no doubt, ffom 'the good sense of Mr. Lewes, when he sees this error, though trifling, taking the whole of his performance collectively, but that he will immediately remedy if; for as it is anExhibition of so much, huniour and strong pointed satire, as cannot fail at once to delight and instruct, so we wish to see the future representations of it as perfect as possible. Delpini’s pleasantry gains, much ground, and Master Braham’s musical powers still astonish the most critical ear. With such auxiliaries, united with, his own exertions to vary, the Entertainment's frequently, Mr. Palmer is likely to long enjoy that liberal support he has already experienced from a generous public.

THE THEATRE,
HAY-MARKET,
Opened a second time last night with Mrs- Inch bald’s admired Comedy of “ TH tell you what!” ' The theatre was tolerably well filled, and by much good company. Almost the whole corps of Dramatis Persona: of "Richmond House were there. In the Prince’s box were his Grace of Richmond, Mrs. Darner, Lord Derby ; and behind them a groupe of Nabobs. The Hon. Mrs. Hobart and a party of friends were on the opposite fide.
The characters of the play were in general well supported ; but the- actors shamefully deficient in the recollection of their parts. Mr. Browne played Major Cypfu.s, for the first time. His action and manners are-genteel, and well suited to the character he perlodated. Mrs. Bulkeley likewise deserves commendation. Parsons, and Miss Farren, were welcomed" to the stage with all the plaudits of approbation they so well deserve. Palmer, in Sir Harry Harlnlefs cut a truly comic appearance," He was a perfect epitome of our modem beaux,Isdth hi name and dress.
The occasional address to the public; spoken by young Sestini, was received with great applause, and does great credit to the genius of the author, It is altered from its original state, and alludes to Mr. Colman’S having been under the necessity of shutting up the house for a short time, from the Managers of: the- winter theatres keeping their louses open so long: He hopes, however, now the competition for public approbation is at an end, to experience the favour ot the town to his summer thtretre,which we heartily wish he may meet with, to the utmost of his wiflics.

HAY-MARKET THEATRE.
"otwithstanding the novelty of a new Theatre, which, doubtless, must , excite the curiosity of the public to a great degree, we could not perceive any visible effect it had on Mr. Colman’s Theatre on Wednesday evening, as a more brilliant audience has not appeared this season.
The favourite musical Comedy of Two to One experienced the niolt-flaucring reception, which, in truth, it amply merited ; as the performers in general exerted themselves with uncommon spirit in their respective characters, for which they are justly entitled- io’rhe warmest eulogiums.
Mrs. Inchbald’s entertaining farce of The Mogul Tale succeeded, and a more laughable afterpiece could not have been found, as it kept the audience in a continual roar throughout the whole. This may, with great propriety, be ranked among the fair author’s first productions, in point of genuine humour. Parsons, in Johnny Atkins, was above all praise. Indeed we may say, without exaggeration, that this favourite Comedian has a greater command ot features than any actor of the present time—his performance was of course inimhable.
Wewitzer, in the Doctor, was as usual ludicrous, to an eminent degree.
Mrs. Brown appeared, for the first time at this Theatre, in the character ot Fanny, originally in the possession of Mrs. Wells, which the performed with infinite credit to herself, and satisfaction to the audience. It is with pleasure we • congratulate • the manager on so valuable an acquisition as this lady will prove to his-theatrical. corps.
The other persons of the drama deserve honourable mention, as they were in general critically just.

Description of Mir. Palmer’s-New Theatre, etear Welidte-uarc. ..
The HoyaRfTheatre; for so it is denominated from the ground oil which it is erected being-within the precincts of apaidee, is constructed of the-' -Very be& materials, iirithtr.expehce nor labour having been "spared to sender it" a perfectly compleat building;. . If there -fie any fault in the architect, it is of the seme riitu're with that iniput-. ed to Sir Jehu 'Vanbrugh, who; in deligoing.the Opera -house, .carried strength and. solidity to such an excess, as if he" intended his edifice to defy the is vages of time.
■Mr. John WiUmdt, the architect, seems to Eave fallen , into the seine, i'f the expression be allowable, laudable error; for, contrary "to the in terest of his profeffion, he -has -been the fabricator of a building, which, from it.s excellent workmanship, will "occasion the proprietors to incur very little expence IN the repairs- during the continuance-of-a century. Mr. John Robinson," of Wellclose-square, and to whom the’ superintend- • afice of the heir work’s at Woolwich was commit-" tid, is surveyor of the Royalty Theatre; in which capacity ha,has (hewn the utmost (kill aud judgment.
- The 'galleries of the Royalty Theatre are infinitely superior to any belonging to the various Theatres in the kingdom. -Being of a semicircular form, "there is not a situation from which' rhe spectators will not have a distinct view of every part of' thb stage, and from the last seat of both galleries the extremity of the building is conspicuously diseemed,! without stooping,-or in any manner varying the natural position of the body. Tfris is an excellence which neither of the houses in the metropolis can boast: and as the advantage of Hie foreign, over our national Theatres, has principally' arisen from the audience part being semicircularly constructed, the designer of the building we aye now describing has judiciously ivaiied himself of such a plan. The upper gallery of the Royalty Theatre will, on the most accurate computation, contain fix hundred and forty persons. The second gallery will contain one thousand ; the pit three hundred and sixty ; the ’Front boxes one hundred and ninety-eight; the fide boxes three hundred and ninety-fix.— The whole length of the Theatre is one hundred and twenty, and the breadth fifty-fix feet.
To the elegant lightness of tke building may be added the beauty of the decorations, in which exquisite taste is displayed. The pillars, which support the' Upper tier of boxes, are not within any of the architectonic orders, but of the fancy kind, and enriched trusses; the middle pillars
partake of the Composite order ; arid the pillars of the lower tier incline to the Doric. Over each box there is an entablature, on which trophies are painted. .The ceiling of the Theatre is highly finished ; there arc four spandrel p-mnels with ornaments in chiaro ofctiro ; in the center a iky, with a border -of oake#s leaves, and the whole is bordered by a galoss'. The Theatre is painted a stone colour, the borders richly gilded. A light crimson paper has been chosen for the boxes, and they are lined with a watered morinejof a correspondent colour; tire border of the paper is of •a light ground, with re legated (colours. The stage box, on his Majeity's fide, is decorated 'with the Royal-Arms, in chiarirofcuro; the opposite box has the arms of the Tower.'
The stage of the Royalty Theatre is considerably longer and wider- than that of the Opera - house; and the scenes are much higher than those at any of the Theatres. The pit passage is" so spacious, as" to be capable of- containing as many persons as the pit itself. There is a most excellent box lobby, abounding with every con. venience, and a drawing room for the ladies, iu
the ftyle of the Opera House. The whole of the boxes are to be illuminated with glass chandeliers, made from a drawing truly elegant. The ceiling •Cftht boxes is considerably lot tier" than that' of either of the houses; arid carc has been taken to r.ender the different avenues as convenient -as possible. What is theatrically termed the Drop Cloth, represents an octagon saloon ; the pillars as the Doric order in two niches are the figures of Prudence and portirude. Above the entablature there are two pannels; on each of which is represented, in basso relievo, "a sacrifice ; .the one to jkbars, the other to Diana. A gallery leading from, .the saloon to other apartments terminates the Hew.
"• Having ■ mentioned the decorations of this. new Theatre, it would be an unpardonable omission' not to pay a tribute to the merit of the artifl, who ■ has-difp’aq-cd so eleganta tafle on the occasion. Mr. Dixon, by whom the organ was painted which is. used in the .Abbey on. the commemoration of Handel, and whose hand is flitting uiflia-. b.le at. the Pantheon, and in the King’s Box at the Opera-house ; he is the person selected to paint all.the scenes at Mr. Palmer’s' Theatre, and" to whom,, whatever there is of novelty, of beauty, or of excellence, in the theatrical parrs, should be ‘‘attributed. On the fourth of June,, being the’ birth-day of oyr moil gracious Sovereign, Mr. Palgier proposes to open the Royalty Theatre; and the summer season not interfering, with the ■ interests of the winter houses, the spirit, of contention cannot possibly sub list where there is hot an object of ’rivalship for which to contend.

Nov. 9, 1787.
THE public are most respectfully informed, that, on Monday next, there will be an entire novelty in the Entertainments at the Royalty Theatre. Besides different Musical Pieces, a new Pantomimic Entertainment will be exhibited ; and, what must afford the most pleasing gratisiiation to the friends of harmony, -Mr. Rannisier will, on Monday, appear at the Royalty Theatre for the first time since his recovery.
N. B. On account of the demand for plates for the new Pantomime Entertainment, those Ladies and Gentlemen who arc desirous of accommodation, are humbly so idled to feud their servants to keep places at sive o’clock.
Mr. Sedgwick is engaged at this Theatre, andwill make his first appearance on Thursday next.
It is now common for people of fashion to traverse the streets of an evening, in order to experience the brilliant display which this metropolis affords, through the general adoption of lamps in the principal shops, warehouses, &c. in both city and suburbs, which, it must be confessed, produce a most striking and beautiful appearance ; and, as we are informed, that Mr. Pidgeon has, for the purpose of reducing the price of these articles, opened a warehouse" at No, 2;t, H'gh-Holborn, we think he can not.fail of. meeting .with the most flattering encouragement. This, indeed, we will venture to prognosticate, not only from their general use, but, likewise from his conducting his business upon so fair and equitable a principle, chat he cannot miss of obtaining the confidence and patronage of the public.

HER .IIAJESTYS THEA TRE.
Mademoiselle Cerito appeared for the last time this seasot on Saturday, and the farewell of the audience was the crowi of her brilliant career, and at the conclusion of every pa4 she had the pleasing task of acknowledgi3g the gift of a fresl bouquet. First came the Gitano, that graceful and dignifeie
relative ofthe C4Atcha, the danseuse displaying at everj mnovement her elasticity, her buoyancy, her absolute commanc oyer the dance, and concluding with that expressive ections the erect drawing up of the figure-an action wvhich seem, to signify the most amiable pride and self-complacency, AR attempt was made by the audience to procure a repetition, but Cerito hae' exerted herself too much to undergo the same fatigue again, and she merely came forward to acknowledge tbe applause. The Gitana was danced between the acts ol the opera, the ballet was an abridged form of the Lac des Fees. In the brilliant yis de deuer in the second act, Verito outshone herself, The advance from the back of the stage with Albert was terrific-a display of the greatest physical power, accompanied by a burst of energy that seemed to ,ave no bounds, but to be compelled to express itself by the rmost reckless movement. In thigs step the youthful spirits, which always give a charm to Cerito's dancing, are beightened to thefieror of a young Bacchante; a Thracian Mtenad xight have advanced thus for the demolition of Orphens. This movement was loudly encored, an,d Cerito gained a fresh round of applause by the good taste she displayedin giving to Albert a portion of the ouquct& that were profusel throwvn. the easy soaring step with which, after thisestonishing movement, she lightly skim3s over the stage, was a most pleasing contrast, exhibiting no less power, but power in a subdued form, not developing itself as before in pure reckless-
e. Guly Stephan, on her entranee, wvas loudlygreeted. She came forward with great force, but presently subsided into the quieter style, dancing with accomplished grace and neat-
irosung to tier perfect intelligence of the steps she was executing, land proceeding without an effort. Hers was the gcntle lyrnc of the "eoetry of motion," Cerito's the wild aithyrambic. The Stynanpos de deux by Cerito and Albert concluded the ballet. It is quite an uniqae dance; the lady appears altogether at the mercy of the gentlemau, who sometimes twirls her round like a teetotumn, sometimes pushes her umcourteously from him, persecuting her round the stage. An inferior dancer could have made nothing of it, but Cerito entered fullv into its humour, going through the sudden evolutions witfi careless strength and naitvet, rendering the dance a joyous sport of youtL She was called for at the fall of the curtain to receive fresh bouquets from the boxes, while the deep thunder of approbation rose from the stalls and pit.
The opera was Lucrezia Borgia, one of the few very modern productions which has kept the stage for three seasons. It has an interest from containing a brliant part of Grisi's, and from being the first opera in which MIario appeared before a London audience. The first chorus, rendered so powerful by the accession of L-ablache, vas encored, and Gr si was caed for at the end, but there wvere portions which went rather heavily. HBR IMAJRSTPS THEATRE.
The new ballet of La File dc i'EriM, produced last night, commenced under circumstances decidedly inauspicious, act the ftrst giving fearful signs that it would be act the last. Nothing went kindly; the hrst dance of Siberian exiles was rather like a parcel of ragamuflin people radely shakmig one snother about, than anything connected with an elegant entertainment, and the auience began so soon to be
erfectly dissatisfied that hisses came from all parts of the honse. Then there was an immense quantity of dull action
rior to the journey of Elizabeth, and then on that jeurney Elizabeth was stopped by a number of uninteresting Cossacks, and all this without a single dance, so that we began to think that as we had lately seen farces without jokes, and comedies without wit, a still bolder experiment was to be tried, andthat we were to be favoured with a ballet withoat any dancing. All this while signs of disapprobation continued,when theCossacks were dispersed by an eveat that threatened to be more serious than any that had preceded. AMademoiselle Guy Stephan, who played Elizabeth, flew to a large wooden cross to save herself from the Cossaeks, and the intention was, that the Volga should burst its banks, overwhelm the Tartars, and carry along in safety the heroine clinging to the cross. Good intentions are, alas! not always fulfilled, and in the present case the unruly waters not only drowned the Cossacks, but knocked down the cross with Idlle. Guy Stephan upon it, who fell screaming violently, and having famted away, was supported by some of the drowned men, who benevolently revived themselves for her assistance. The waters having accomplished this lawless work, fapped their canvass waves to the lamps in ghastly triumph, while the curtain, .which under such circumstances should have descended with double rapidity, and concealed the scene of horror, refused to fall more than half-way, mutinously waving backwarda and forwards, amid the wailings of scene-shifters, and the sibilations of the public. At last down it came, ri-htinto the middle of the waters, which having done all the mischief they could, had coagulated themselves into an inert lump in the front of the Stage. So ended act the first, and a fearful pause followed, which lasted so long that the audience began to shout for Laporte. Mlatters looked
most unpromising; here was a public out of humour, the first danseuse in al probability too frightened to go on (if not hurt), and machinery performing every function but, that for which it was designed. Nothing looked so like condemnation. But no ! The fates were not so spiteful as they appeared, or indeed rather seem to have provided this gloomy commencement to make the conclusion more bnlliant. Mademoiselle Guy Stephan came on, in the second act, look-ing as well as evez-, in spite of the tumble, and was generously greeted by every one in the house. Then she recounted the story of herjourney to the Czar in pantomimic action with so much feeling and animation, and eJhibited so much more dramatic power than she has hitherto been supposed to possess, that she drew down the warmest applause. The ballet concluded with a very splendid ball-room in the Czar's Palace, in which Guy Stephan, Alesdemoiselles Pierson and Keppler, M. Guerra, &c., introduced some very pretty and characteristic dances, and the curtain fell (qmckly enough this time) amid unanimous approbation. Thus did a piece which began so mournfully come to an honourable concusion, and with a little curtailment is very likely to become a favourite, an4 gain an accession of public favour to MIademoiselle Guy Stephan, whose energy and presence of moind in saving a piece under such very trying circumstances cannot he too highly commended.
Annia Botena was the opera last night, Grisi, Rlbini, Lablache, Granchi, and E. Gisi sustaining the principal characters. The vicissitudes of the ballet have occupied the space that might have been devoted to a more detailed aCcount of the opera, and ns it is so well known, it will be sufficient briefly to state that the great artis,ts cted and sung with their usual excellence, and that the opera was well received tbroughout.
