flER MVAJESTrY. TiHF,TRE.
The Opera was completely crowded on Saturday night, the performance being on Uliovanni and the new ballet of XAI,na.
The ballet has been shortened by the omission of a warriors' dance by the corps de ballte, and Cerito's Spanish dance, both in the fourth tableau. These omissions are judicious, as they lighten the piece in its least effectiva part, and at a time wheu the audience begin to think theyhave remained long enough. A further improvement may be made by shortening the interval between the second and third tabieensr if, indeed,this can be effected so as to leave zime for the change of dresses for the ball scene. Alma is such a beautiful ballet that it is worth every possible attempt to bring it to perfection. Not only is it the most brilliant spectacle that has for many years been seen on the Opera stage, but the three principalsas have never been exceeded for invention and beauty. The first of these, the valse defascination,whenthestrange little demon and the daughter of fire lure the German maidens to dance, was composed by Perrot, and the conception is worthy of a Ketsch. Perrot has made a finished portrait of the frolicsome Mephistophiles; the saucy turn of his hat, the insol- nt strut the half-mischievous, half-terrnfic look of defiance which he casts round him, produce an effect which the odd expression of his features tends greatly to heighten. There is a fine want of courtesy in his mode of asking the three reluctant damsels to dance, in his holding up his finger with a sneer of superiority, fally aware that come they must. But his power is incompletew thout the aid of his fair coadjutor, thefile du feu-Cerito. Lie must invite her to join in the temptation, and then it is irresistible. Here the pvus brings out very prominently an element in Ceritos dancing with which the public have been less familiar. It is not the exhibiion of physical force, but of power subdued down to its easiest exoression. Hers is a sort of quiet witchery; it iuplayful yet malicious-atmost demoniac. Nothing can be more perfect as a dramatic pas both in its idea and the delicacy with which it is worked out, than this ra!se defascination. The second pas-the pas de trois-by Cerito, Camille, and M. Desplaces, was invented by Cerito, and the slow part of it shows the most refined perception of the beautiful in the arrangement of groups. While Uerito has of course reserved for herself the principal place, dropping in the most varied attitudes in the arms of Desplaces, her disposition of the third figure (Camille), who, separate from the other two, always completes the group, is most elegant. The third pasthe pas de trohs-by Perrot, Guy Stephan, and Fleury, was invented by Perrot. This dance is excellently adapted for the display of those rising danscuses, Guy St phan and Fleury, the former bounding' along with energy and animation, her facebeaming with excitement, the tsll figure of the latter springing with equal force, yet appatrently less excited, and more calmly trusting to her physical strength. These tiree pas are enough to iusnsre the success of any ballet, even if not produced with such superb decorations as Alma.

ST. JAMES'S THEATRE.
The Polka, that wonderful dance which has so suddenly shot up into the meridian of popularity, dividing the public attention with the most vital questions of state, has at last found its way to this lHttle house, which, by the by, is its natural home, for from the French it is that we have caught this Terpsichorean infection. Much of the extraordinary sensation which the dance has produced we think may be attributed tothe fine state of vagueness in which it at present exists, for of the entire cargo of dancing-masters who immediately rushed to the Tower-stairs, on the first wafting of the name to British ears, not one but has returned sole possessor of the real Polka, and denouncing all athers as spurious concoctions and utterly un-Bohemian. At present our knowledge iS limited to the fact that brass heels are an indispensible inzredient, and that the steps are more or less approaches to the dancing which usually accompanies the song of " Jim Orow." The little piece in which the Polka was introduced last night at this theatre, danced by Madame Albert and a Mademoiselle Forgeot, is entitled La P2wes de la Poldk, and is as absurd in its purport as the subject deserves. It represents the court of some provincial town as occupied with the trial of an action, brought by the citizens of the said tovn against a certain instractor of the Polka, on the plea of its turning the heads of all the female inhabitants, and causing the terror of all respectable fathers of families as to the moral welfare of their charges. The cause is regularly tried before the lieiuenant-gene-al. and ileaded on the plaintiff's side by an old lawyer, whose distaste to the dance arises from his l%meness, and on the defendant's by a plump young advocate w'ho is himself under. tuition to acquire the incriminated Polka. After citing various ancient authorities in favour of dancing generaUy, he calls upon the coujt not to decide until they have themselves seen the Polka. The request is granted, and forthwith two ladies in full Polka costume, armed with brass heels, enter the court, oie of whom, to the astoiahment of the judge, he finds to be his wife. The Polka, is danced in the openi arena, and its fascination is so powerful that ere long the whole court in wig and gown are seen jigging to the measure. This, of course, decides the affair, and the curtain descends on the trinumph of the Polka over the moral prejudices of boiurgeois and p res de famile. It is needless to say that a vociferous enore was the result, and it was accomntnied by the projection of one or two bouquets. The Po 'which was danced on this occasion differs from all those we have hitherto seen but whether it be the genuine one, let M. Conlon and a jury of native Pohemians decide.

BOYAL CRORAL SOCIETY.
At last night's performance of Berlioz's Faust Sir Joseph Barnby's choir were again in splendid condition, and their singing reached a very high standard of cecellenco throughout; the dance of peasants,the burlesque " Amen " for male voices,the choral accompaniments to Faust's dream, and other sections were sung in a manner which left little to be desired. The orchestral numbers were less completely satisfactory, though the usual encores were claimed for the Hangarian march and the dance of sylphs. The latter was taken at an unusually rapid pace, and at the close of the fne Easter hymn the flute passages were very roughly played. The defect was atoned for by the remarkably good performance of the same instramEnts in the dance of will-o'-the-visps, the best played of the orchestral movements.
Originally intended for a mezzo-soprano, the part of Margaret is not one of those which best suit Miss Anna Williams; the powers of this distinguished artist are to a great extent wasted on music which requires nothing more than tenderness and simplicity of expression, and which lies outside the best part of her voice. She was suffering from hoarseness and an apology was circulated in the hall on this account; nevertheless, she sang the ballad of the King of Thule with great taste and' artistic feeling, and displayed much dramatic power in the trio. Mr. Iver Ll'Kay sang extremely well in the whole sce;ne of the trio, but for the rest he failed to impazt much effect to the title part ; Mr. Henschel sang the music of Mephistopheles with his usual dramatic power and humour, making the most of the " flea " song and of the mocking serenade, the latter of which had to be repeated. Mr. Robert Grice was efficient as Brander.

In August, that month of depression in matters theatrical, it is not surprising to see such houses as still find themselves in the happy position of being able to keep open their doors making efforts to add to the attractiveness of their entertainments. Thanks to that clever and amusing jumble of song annt dance called Atorocco Bound, the Shaftesbury Theatre is one of the lucky ones, and no better fillip to arouse the interest of its patrons could have been provided than the new dance introduced last night by Miss Letty Lind, entitled " The Surprise Dance." It is stated to be an imitation uf the serpentine dance which has recently acquired so much popularity, with novel ideas and fancies of Miss Lied's own. The result does credit to her in every way, and the reception it met wi'th proves that this dance will do much to draw the public to the theatre even in this dull season. The play itself has been brought well up to date. Tho topical songs deal in bumorous fashion w-ith the recent development of superabundant energy in tho I-ouse of Commons, and Mr. J. L. Shine's song descriptive of Irelandi under Home Rule was loudly encored, as was also his duet with Mr. Danby giving imitations of music-hall singers. Mr. George Grossmith, jUn., in the small part allotted to him, makes a decided hit. Considering the amount of vitality there is about it, tbere can be but little doubt that .torocco Bouad will continue to hold the favour of the public for some time to come.

CLASSICAL DANCES AT THEDORS
The latest exponent of what goes by the name of “ classical dancing " is Frau Gertrud von Leth, who gave a very artistic little entertainment on Thursday in the somewhat incongruous surroundings of the Dote Galleries. It was left for her to dance a whole sonata, and in choosing Beethoven’s op. 28 for illustration she was quite wise, for the scherzo apd funeral march almost of themselves suggest the art of gesture, and the variations of the first movement allow of sufficient variety of movement. The danger might have stopped from some design of Canova or Flax man, so much of the classicism of the 18th ceptury ;s suggested ip hor girlish figure and her facial type. Willa she employs very much the game kinds of gesture and steps as those of Miss Maud Allan, with the unchanging smilo of Miss Isadora Duncap,sho has some of the forme" artist’s wonderful power of translating the phrases of the music ipto steps of her donee so literally that in some of Chopin’s studies she seemed to be dancing the right notes while the pianist was certainly playing she wrong. Her sense pf rhythm is very highly developed. Her costumes are in excellent taste, though there is, of course, not very much of them; and in some of the waltzes of Brahms, op. 39, she manipulated a scarf with remarkable skill. Eight boys from the London College for Choristers sang between the dances, but did not add much to the attraction of the entertainment. If Frau von Leth ia to have a success in London, sho must secure the services of a moro competent pianii t than the lady who essayed the task with very moderate success.

At her 18th.special-matinee, given yesterday, Miss Maud Allan interpreted for the first time four movements of Tchaikovslcy’a " Casse-Noisette ” suits—tho reed-pipe danco, the Arabian dance, tho danco of the sugar-plum fairy, and tho Valse des Fleurs. They were all good opportunities for her beautiful, artless art. Fundamentally there is something childish and untutored about Mid? Allan’s performances which seems to guarantee their spontaneity. The music speaks through a new and lovely instrument; there appear to be no rules, no science to come between the music and its expression in movement and gesture. And though we may feel sometimes that tho music does not always affect ns in precisely tbs way it affects Miss Allan (there is a passage, for instance, in Chopin’s funeral march, another of yesterday’s dances, which inspires us with totally different feelings from those expressed), it is clear that she is extraordinarily sensitive to tho music, besides inevitably graceful and beautiful in translating her emotions into movement. About the " Casse-Noisctto ” suite opinions and feelings could hardly differ. Tho only possible criticism would be that the similarity of the sugar-plum fairy to the Mendelssohn " Spring Song ” might argue a poverty of invention; but that would hardly be fair, for invention is not in the case. Tho movements of the sugar-plum fairy are a little those of the maiden in the spring meadenya, but sho is not the same person; just as the Arabian dance introduced some of tbo Salome movements, while the two were widely different. Perhaps Miss Allan was at her best in the Valse des Fleurs. The music of it seemed to intoxicate
a delightful performance, full of fun and fantasy, and of tho grace and beauty which this dancer see ins realty unable to avoid. Miss Margaret Cooper’s songs are a most important and popular feature of these special m atinees, and the bioscope comes at the end.

MRS. WILLIAM JAMES’S DANCE.
The Duke and Duchess of Connaught, Princess Patricia of Connaught, Princess Christian and Princess Victoria of Schleswig-Holstein honoured Mr. and Mrs. Willliam James by being present at their dance last night at 38, Bryanston-square: The Duchess of Connaught, Princess Patricia of Connaught, Princess Christian and Princess Victoria of Schleswig-Holstein dined with Mr. and Mrs. James before the dance, and among those invited to dinner to meet their Royal Highnesses iwere the AustroHungarian Ambassador, Prince Louis of Orleans, the Earl of Clanwilliam, Earl Somers, Lord Herbert Vane-Tempest, Lord Richard Nevill, and Lord Gerald Wellesley. The Duke of Connaught came on to Bryanston-square after the Army Service Corps dinner at the Hotel Cecil.
Dancing took place m the large drawing room on the first floor, the fireplaces and window recesses being filled with pink and white hydrangeas, while in the retiring rooms masses of rambler roses were arranged with much artistic effect. The balcony was covered in with pink and white bunting, and made a cool resting place between the dances. Supper was served downstairs in the dining room and also in the small garden, which was' covered in and transformed into a bower of crimson and- pink rambler roses, while numerous electric lamps, half hidden in masses of pink and white flowers, were suspended from the roof. About 300 guests took part: in the dancing, which was kept up until an early hour.

The Arts
I-1l
Salzburg's First-Rate
Idomeneo
FROM OUR SPECIAL CORRESPONDENT I
SALZBURG. AUG. 21
There was a time, not so long ago, when Idomeneo was scarcely known even to Mozart lovers; the performance on Saturday at Salzburg showed most graphically what a popular opera it has become. For after the premiere itself (Figaro) and Karajan's Trovatore this splendid production of Mozart's first great opera was on all counts-socially and musically-one of the highlights of the present festival.
The lion's share of this success must go to the brilliant youdg conductor Peter Maag, who is certainly one of the finest Mozart interpreters of our xeneration. The Vienna Philharmonic played with fire and enthusiasm, but also with the greatest delicacy. One of the most delightful effects was that of Mr. Maag's harpsichord playing: he not only played the secco recitatives with real imagination but during the accompanied recitatives he artfully played the harpsichord with his right (or left) hand, which in soft passages added a most intriguing sound-musically very effective and historically correct-to the soft sheen *-f the Vienna Philharmonic strings.
The production (Paul Hager) and the sets (Stefan Hlawa) were very close to those of the recent revival at the Maggio Musicale, where Mr. Maag also conducted and Mr. Hlawa was responsible for the visual element; but the Salzburg production was in overy way superior to that of Florence. Waldemar Kmentt as Idomeneo sang better than we have beard him for several years; his voice 'bas taiken on a darker colour, but it still rings over the orchestra with youth;ful vigour. Elisabeth GrUmmner as Electra brought the house down with her C minor aria, the climax of which includes two cruelly difficult runs to top C; and Pilar Lorengar as Ilia displayed a warm, sympathetic voice and a fine sense of phrasing. Against these three principals, Mr. Ernst HaRfinger. as Idamantes, was perhaps not the best choice vocally, thouth he sang with great cultiv ation, especially in the piano passages, It is a pity that Signor Renato Capecohi's talents were not put to better use, here in Salzburg: for Arbaces is reallly a very minor part.
ADMIRABLE CHORUS WORK A special word of praise should go to the Vienna State Opera Chorus, which sang with admirable precision throughout. Most of the ballets (wvhich made the production last year so long) have been cut out of this year's Idomeneo; and no one missed them.
In view of this really first-rate performance, it was a pity that Salzburg continued to use the version prepared by Herr Bernhard Paumgartner. The layman, unfamiliar with this wonderful score, can hardly imagine the cruel cuts. the senseless additions (bars repeated to enable the chorus to get on or off stage in time), the unmusical graftings in the recitatives and, above all, the changes in sequence in Act 111, with which this Paumgartner version is burdened. By destroying Mozart's beautiful sequence of keys in Act III, Herr Paumgartner was forced to compose whole sections, lifting pieces out of Electra's recnative before her great aria and giving them to Idomeneo; and instead of the powerful chorus, " 0 voto tremendo ". with its sinister muted trumpets and timpani, leading. bv way of a thrilling crescendo, to the famous " pizzicato " chorus, the latter was placed before " 0 voto " and the whole dramatic sequence ruined
It seems really beyond belief that anyone would wan.t to improve on Mozart's sense of timing, his innate dramatic flair. No doubt the fact that Herr Paumgartner is also president of the Salzburg Festival was a prime motive in using his version of Idomeneo; but, whatever the reason, we surely have every right to expect unadulterated Mozart in the Salzburg Festival, and it is high time that someone prepared a version of this great opera that corresponds, in its essen-tials, to the score Mozart wrote.
Another high-point of the present festival turned out to be the suest appearance of the ballot from the Theatre Royal de la Monnaie in Brussels. Using the Vienna Philharmonic Orchestra, conducted by Pierre Boulez, the famous company gave us brinliant performances of Stravinsky's Les Noces and Le Sacre du Printemps; the choreography of Le Sacre was quite a shock to the Salzburg audience, whose knowledge of Freud is perhaps not that of their Gallic counterparts. But having swallowed the often drastically realistic actions on the stage, they v-gorously applauded at the end. Ballet in Austria has, for the past 100 years, been something of a step-child; and we have not seen-even in the Vienna State Opera-anything like the precision and beauty of the Brussels group.
MUCH-LOVED INSTITUTION
Mozart's Cos) was the subject of a recent article in these columns; and 100 years ago no one could have predicted that this miraculous but then misunderstood score would one day be the opera of the Salzburg Festival. For, even m-lore than Figaro, Coei seems to our generation to sum up all that is the essence of the Mozartian spirit. The rightly famous Salzburg production (Schwarzkopf, Ludwig, Hermann Prey as Guglielmo, Waldemar Kmentt as Ferrando, Graziella Sciutti as Despine, and Carl DMnch as Don Alfonso), conducted by Karl Bohrm with the Vienna Philharmonic Orchestra, and produced by Gunther Rennert, has become a much-loved institution.
If one might prefer a more elegant Don Alfonso-Franco Calabrese was, for* some reason, not a success when he took over the part some years ago here-and if this year's production sometimes verged on the mannered, nevertheless it is hard to imagine a more perfect female cast than Schwarzkopf, Ludwig, and Sciutti. It will interest many readers that E.M.I. is preparing a new stereophonic recording of this opera in London this autumn, with Mesdatnes Schwarzkopf and Ludwig, and to be conducted by Karl Bohm with the Philharinonia Orchestra. There is, happily, no doubt that Mozart's Cosi has become not only an artistic but also a commercial sueces&.

| Rodney Miines's opera choice: War and Peace |
Coliseum, London WC2, Oct 27 & Nov 1; in repertory until Nov 28 (020-7632 8300) PROKOFIEV'S OPERATIC version of Tolstoy 's novel is one of the greatest
challenges that any opera company can face: it is four hours long, there are 13 separate scenes and more than 100 named roles from the lead characters to
assorted partisans harrying Napoleon (above) and his army during the retreat from Moscow. Prokofiev was himself harried by the Soviet thought-police as he struggled through endless revisions to make his work conform to the tenets of socialist realism and the demands for Stalin - wors hip . He died on the same day as Stalin in 1953 without seeing his masterpiece staged. Given the circumstances, it is amazing that the opera should be as coherent as it is, but the glorious runes and orchestration, sumptuously romantic and bitingly astringent by turns , carry it through . ENO has a strong cast for Tim Albery 's new production: Simon Keenlyside as Prince Andrei, Willard White as Kutuzov (the Stalin role), Sandra Zeltzer as Natasha, John Daszak as Pierre, John Graham-Hall and Susan Parry as the Kuragins, and Peter Sidhom as Napoleon. The designs are by Hildegard Bechtler and Ana Jebens, and Paul Daniel conducts.

GLYNDEBOURNE
OPERA
" ALCESTE "
Akcet ..... ........ MAGDA LASzIO Admtte .............. RICHAD LEwis Le Grand Pr2tre ........... RAlMtDO TorxEs Hercule ............ THtos HEMsLEy Evandre ........... DERtmr TIoy
Conductor: Virruto Gut
FROM OUR MUSIC CRMC
LEWES, JuN 13
Gluck's Alceste is never likely to become a stock opera and perhaps not even to attain the currency of the same composer's Orpheus. All the more important therefore that an organization
like Glyndebourne should undertake to ! mount it. I
Everyone knows of its historical importance, and the famous preface has fittingly been printed in the programme book this year. But an opera cannot keep itself alive on history alone: it must have sufficient musico-dramatic vitality in itself. All Gluck's reformed operas, from Orpheus to /phigenia, however much of the old opera seria still clinfs to them, have this viability. If last year s production of Alceste demonstrated so much, this year's revival in a much more vivid performance amply confirms it.
The strength of this performance is the consistent style in which Greek classicism is seen through the eyes of French classical drama. Sir Hugh Casson's monumental sets, containing the tremendous archaic statue of Apollo, are similarly not a copy but a view of antiquity. Professor Ebert has provided exactly the right amount of movement, notably for the chorus, to ensure that Gluck's break with eighteenth-century convention, boldly declared by the choral invocation of the opemng, carries its intended dramatic force, and everywhere the light and shade of the action is faithfully depicted on the stage.
Miss Laszlo's interpretation of Alceste has acquired both depth and strength since last year, and it was not merely " Divinites du Styx " but each of her big arias that bore the opera along. Her voice may not possess great variety of colour but it has a purity of line that is invaluable to Gluck and to the classical style. Mr. Richard Lewis, with sufficient heroic ring in his tone, sang with greater freedom and conviction. Apollo, whether as priest or deus ex machina, was sung by Mr. Raimundo Torres, who has a weighty voice for the dual role: he seemed dramatically uncomfortable as the priest, external to the part so to speak, but as the god conveyed authority. Mr. Thomas Hemsley treated the equivocal character of Hercule with the right touch, and the Evandre of Mr. Dermot Troy, like the other smal parts, was admirably done.
The opera was sung in French and in the French version. The French language is difficult for an international cast, but if distinctive French sonorities were generally absent it can at any rate be said that Gluck's music was originally written to an Italian text and only adapted to French in 1776. Mr. Vittorio Gui's hand on the music was suavis in modo but fortis in re. Hence the style of the result.
